\section*{Text Files}\label{text-files}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Text Files}

\markright{Text Files}

\subsection*{What text files?}\label{what-text-files}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{What text files?}

A text file is any file containing only readable characters: -
Characters being letters, numbers\ldots{} - As well as other symbols and
exotic symbols - English and other languages

A text file may letters, numbers, and other symbols, in English or other
languages.

In a text file:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{Symbols} can have special meanings, e.g.~math \$ A =
  \pi r\^{}2\$
\item
  \textbf{Code} in special syntax, e.g.~HTML code
\item
  Writing/data in various \textbf{structures}, e.g.~text in paragraphs
  or tables:
\end{itemize}

{\def\LTcaptype{none} % do not increment counter
\begin{longtable}[]{@{}lll@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
Column 1 & Column 2 & Column 3 \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\ldots{} & \ldots{} & \ldots{} \\
\ldots{} & \ldots{} & \ldots{} \\
\end{longtable}
}

A scientific article is an example of a text file, where you may
encounter math equations and symbols with special meanings. It may be a
web page with HTML code, which follows a special syntax defined by the
W3 consortium. It may contain descriptions in paragraphs and data in
tabulated structures.

\subsection*{What is not a text file?}\label{what-is-not-a-text-file}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{What is not a text file?}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Binary files

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    JPEG or GIF pictures
  \item
    MP3 audio files
  \item
    Binary document formats, e.g.~PPT
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Binary files require special programs
\end{itemize}

Text files can be viewed and edited by any text editor, e.g.~Notepad on
PC, TextEdit on Mac, and vi on Linux/Unix, etc. But binary files require
special software to access them.

In this lecture, we focus on processing text files.

\section*{Reading a file}\label{reading-a-file}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Reading a file}

\markright{Reading a file}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  To read and print the content of a text file:
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{f }\OperatorTok{=} \BuiltInTok{open}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"months.txt"}\NormalTok{)}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(f.read())}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

The example reads the entire content of a file. The \texttt{open}
function creates a file object (a way of getting at the contents of the
file), which is then stored in the variable f.~\texttt{f.read()} tells
the file object to read the full contents of the file, and return it as
a string.

\section*{Reading a file by smaller
pieces}\label{reading-a-file-by-smaller-pieces}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Reading a file by smaller pieces}

\markright{Reading a file by smaller pieces}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  To read and print character by character:
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{f }\OperatorTok{=} \BuiltInTok{open}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"months.txt"}\NormalTok{)}
\BuiltInTok{next} \OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ f.read(}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{)}
\ControlFlowTok{while} \BuiltInTok{next} \OperatorTok{!=} \StringTok{""}\NormalTok{:}
    \BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\BuiltInTok{next}\NormalTok{)}
    \BuiltInTok{next} \OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ f.read(}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

The example here reads a file a character at a time. This calls the
\texttt{read()} function with parameter value 1, which specifies the
maximum number of characters to read from the file. When it reaches the
end of the file, the \texttt{f.read()} returns an empty ``\,'' and ends
the while loop.

\section*{Reading a file by line}\label{reading-a-file-by-line}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Reading a file by line}

\markright{Reading a file by line}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  To read and print line by line:
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{f }\OperatorTok{=} \BuiltInTok{open}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"months.txt"}\NormalTok{)}
\BuiltInTok{next} \OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ f.readline()}
\ControlFlowTok{while} \BuiltInTok{next} \OperatorTok{!=} \StringTok{""}\NormalTok{:}
    \BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\BuiltInTok{next}\NormalTok{)}
    \BuiltInTok{next} \OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ f.readline}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

It is very common to read a file one text line at a time. Here the code
calls the \texttt{readline()} function to read a line at a time. When it
reaches the end of file, \texttt{readline()} returns an empty ``\,'' and
terminates the loop.

\section*{Reading all lines in a
file}\label{reading-all-lines-in-a-file}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Reading all lines in a file}

\markright{Reading all lines in a file}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  To read all lines:
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{f }\OperatorTok{=} \BuiltInTok{open}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"months.txt"}\NormalTok{)}
\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ month }\KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ f.readlines():}
   \BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Month "} \OperatorTok{+}\NormalTok{ month.strip())}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Another alterantive, shown here, is to read a file and load all text
lines: - The \texttt{readlines()} method reads all the lines in a file
and returns them as a Python list. - You can create a loop to go through
the list of lines: - The \texttt{strip()} method removes leading and
trailing characters, i.e.~spaces by default.

\section*{Writing to files}\label{writing-to-files}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Writing to files}

\markright{Writing to files}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Default mode of open() is to read
\item
  To open a file to write:
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{f }\OperatorTok{=} \BuiltInTok{open}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"awesomenewfile.txt"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"w"}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Parameter:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \texttt{r} to read
  \item
    \texttt{w} to write (overwrite)
  \item
    \texttt{w+} to write (and create if file does not exist)
  \item
    \texttt{a} to append
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

With the option ``w'', you can open a file to write. All existing
content in the files will be overwritten. If you only want to add new
lines to the existing content, use option \texttt{a} to append.
\texttt{w+} create the file if it does not exist yet. \texttt{r} is the
default option to read from a file.

\section*{Writing to files}\label{writing-to-files-1}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Writing to files}

\markright{Writing to files}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Writing text to file:
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{f }\OperatorTok{=} \BuiltInTok{open}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"names.txt"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"w+"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{f.}\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(“John”)}
\NormalTok{f.write(“Luke\textbackslash{}n”)}
\NormalTok{f.write(“Matt\textbackslash{}n”)}
\NormalTok{f.close()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

It is a good practice to close the file in the end with the
\texttt{close()} method. The \texttt{print} method writes the text and
adds a new line character at the end by default. The \texttt{write()}
does not do that by default so if you need a new line character and you
have to include the \texttt{\textbackslash{}n} (newline character)
manually.

\section*{More examples}\label{more-examples}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{More examples}

\markright{More examples}

Here are a few more examples to recap what we have discussed.

To open a text file:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{fh }\OperatorTok{=} \BuiltInTok{open}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"hello.txt"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"r"}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

To read the entire content of a text file:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{fh }\OperatorTok{=} \BuiltInTok{open}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"hello.txt"}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{"r"}\NormalTok{)}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{ fh.read()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

To read one line at a time:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{fh }\OperatorTok{=} \BuiltInTok{open}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"hello.txt"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"r"}\NormalTok{)}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{ fh.readline()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

To read a list of lines:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{fh }\OperatorTok{=} \BuiltInTok{open}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"hello.txt."}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"r"}\NormalTok{)}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{ fh.readlines()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

To write a text line to a file, and replace its existing content:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{fh }\OperatorTok{=} \BuiltInTok{open}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"hello.txt"}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{"w"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{write(}\StringTok{"Hello World"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{fh.close()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

To write mutliple text lines to a file:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{fh }\OperatorTok{=} \BuiltInTok{open}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"hello.txt"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"w"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{lines\_of\_text }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ [}\StringTok{"a line of text"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"another line of text"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"a third line"}\NormalTok{]}
\NormalTok{fh.writelines(lines\_of\_text)}
\NormalTok{fh.close()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

To append a text file to a file:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{fh }\OperatorTok{=} \BuiltInTok{open}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"hello.txt"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"a"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{write(}\StringTok{"Hello World again"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{fh.close}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

And finally, to close a file after you are done with it:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{fh }\OperatorTok{=} \BuiltInTok{open}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"hello.txt"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"r"}\NormalTok{)}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{ fh.read()}
\NormalTok{fh.close()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\section*{Strings Processing and
Statistics}\label{strings-processing-and-statistics}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Strings Processing and Statistics}

\markright{Strings Processing and Statistics}

Now that we can read/write text files, there are a lot we can do to
process data in the text.

\section*{Example problem}\label{example-problem}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Example problem}

\markright{Example problem}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Voting for favoured radish:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    What is the most popular?
  \item
    What are the least popular?
  \item
    Did anyone vote twice?
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

In \texttt{radishsurvey.txt}:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{Evie Pulsford {-} April Cross}
\NormalTok{Matilda Condon {-} April Cross}
\NormalTok{Samantha Mansell {-} Champion}
\NormalTok{geronima trevisani {-} cherry belle}
\NormalTok{Alexandra Shoebridge {-} Snow Belle}
\NormalTok{...}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

The example here is a survey about the types of radish people like. In
this survey data of radish votes, each vote is recorded in the format
of:

\texttt{Name\ of\ Person} followed by the \texttt{Name\ of\ Radish}

with a \texttt{-} to separate the two.

\subsection*{Reading survey}\label{reading-survey}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{Reading survey}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Read and split:
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ line }\KeywordTok{in} \BuiltInTok{open}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"radishsurvey.txt"}\NormalTok{):}
\NormalTok{   line }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ line.strip()}
\NormalTok{   parts }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ line.split(}\StringTok{" {-} "}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{   name }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ parts[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{]}
\NormalTok{   vote }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ parts[}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{]}
   \BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(name }\OperatorTok{+} \StringTok{" voted for "} \OperatorTok{+}\NormalTok{ vote)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

First, we read the file one line at a time with a \texttt{for} loop.

The \texttt{strip()} method removes leading and trailing spaces in a
text line, and then we \texttt{split()} the text line into a list of two
parts, name and radish, using the delimiter \texttt{-}.

\subsection*{On list and split}\label{on-list-and-split}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{On list and split}

In Python, you can assign a list of values to multiple variables:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{a, b, c }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ [}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{]}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(b)}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(a)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

When you split something into a list in Python, you can assign the
result directly to multiple variables:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{name, cheese, cracker }\OperatorTok{=} \StringTok{"Fred,Jarlsberg,Rye"}\NormalTok{.split(}\StringTok{","}\NormalTok{)}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(cheese)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\subsection*{Counting votes}\label{counting-votes}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{Counting votes}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Counting votes for a specific radish:
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Counting votes for White Icicle..."}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{count }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{0}
\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ line }\KeywordTok{in} \BuiltInTok{open}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"radishsurvey.txt"}\NormalTok{):}
\NormalTok{   line }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ line.strip()}
\NormalTok{   name, vote }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ line.split(}\StringTok{" {-} "}\NormalTok{)}
   \ControlFlowTok{if}\NormalTok{ vote }\OperatorTok{==} \StringTok{"White Icicle"}\NormalTok{:}
\NormalTok{      count }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ count }\OperatorTok{+} \DecValTok{1}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(count)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Back to the radish survey.

The code here counts the number of votes for a specific radish such as
\texttt{White\ Icicle}. It initializes the \texttt{count} as 0, goes
through every vote line in the data, and, whenever there is a match,
adds 1 to the count.

This does the job well. However, if you want to count different kinds of
radish, the code is not very reusable.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  General function for counting votes:
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ count\_votes(radish):}
    \BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Counting votes for "} \OperatorTok{+}\NormalTok{ radish }\OperatorTok{+} \StringTok{"..."}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    count }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{0}
    \ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ line }\KeywordTok{in} \BuiltInTok{open}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"radishsurvey.txt"}\NormalTok{):}
\NormalTok{        line }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ line.strip()}
\NormalTok{        name, vote }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ line.split(}\StringTok{" {-} "}\NormalTok{)}
        \ControlFlowTok{if}\NormalTok{ vote }\OperatorTok{==}\NormalTok{ radish:}
\NormalTok{            count }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ count }\OperatorTok{+} \DecValTok{1}
    \ControlFlowTok{return}\NormalTok{ count}

\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(count\_votes(}\StringTok{"White Icicle"}\NormalTok{))}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(count\_votes(}\StringTok{"Daikon"}\NormalTok{))}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(count\_votes(}\StringTok{"Sicily Giant"}\NormalTok{))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

We can put the code into a function called \texttt{count\_votes()}, with
a \texttt{radish} parameter to tell the function what to count on when
we call it. Now the function can be called to count different radish
types.

\subsection*{Counting ALL Votes}\label{counting-all-votes}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{Counting ALL Votes}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  What if you don't know the radish names
\item
  Count all of them in the survey?
\item
  Use a dictionary to keep track to every radish
\end{itemize}

Still, calling the function requires your knowledge of what radish might
have been voted for in the survey. To make the vote-counting task
easier, perhaps it is better to count all the votes in the survey and
whatever radish appears in the survey will be included in the final
counts.

One specific data structure we will be using is \texttt{dictionary},
which will help us keep track of radish names (as keys) and their counts
(as values).

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# create an empty dictionary}
\NormalTok{counts }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ \{\}}

\CommentTok{\# go through the survey and add counts for each radish}
\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ line }\KeywordTok{in} \BuiltInTok{open}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"radishsurvey.txt"}\NormalTok{):}
\NormalTok{    line }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ line.strip()}
\NormalTok{    name, vote }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ line.split(}\StringTok{" {-} "}\NormalTok{)}
    \ControlFlowTok{if}\NormalTok{ vote }\KeywordTok{not} \KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ counts:}
        \CommentTok{\# Initial value for a radish, if it is in the count yet}
\NormalTok{        counts[vote] }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{1}
    \ControlFlowTok{else}\NormalTok{:}
        \CommentTok{\# Increment the vote count}
\NormalTok{        counts[vote] }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ counts[vote] }\OperatorTok{+} \DecValTok{1}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(counts) }
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

First, in line 2, we create an empty dictionary to keep track of votes.

Then, starting in line 5, it goes through the survey and adds 1 to a
count associated with each type of radish.

If the radish has not been voted before the current vote, it has not
been counted and the key (radish name) is not in the dictionary yet. In
this case, as shown in code line 10, we need to add the key and set its
initial value to 1.

If the radish has been voted already, simply add 1 to the existing count
(line 13).

In the end, we use the \texttt{print()} function to output data in the
dictionary structure.

\subsection*{Output}\label{output-1}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{Output}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Use Python module pprint (pretty) print:
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ pprint }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ pprint}
\NormalTok{pprint(counts)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

If the format of the \texttt{print()} output does not look good, you may
consider the \texttt{pprint}, or pretty print, module in Python.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Print the votes in a custome format:
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ name }\KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ counts:}
\NormalTok{    count }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ counts[name]}
    \BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(name }\OperatorTok{+} \StringTok{": "} \OperatorTok{+} \BuiltInTok{str}\NormalTok{(count))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Or you may consider rendering the output using the loop.

\subsection*{Data Cleaning}\label{data-cleaning}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{Data Cleaning}

Problem in the final counts:

\begin{verbatim}
Red King: 1
red king: 3
White Icicle: 1
Cherry Belle: 2
daikon: 4
Cherry  Belle: 1
...
\end{verbatim}

Shown here is an example output of the program we have created.

In the final result of counts, did you notice that capital
\texttt{Red\ King} and lowercase \texttt{red\ king},
\texttt{Cherry\ Belle} and \texttt{Cherry\ Belle} (with two spaces in
the middle) are treated as different radishes?

Apparently, when people vote for their favorite radish, they enter the
names in slightly format formats. Now that we know about the dirty data,
we should unify (normalize) them by removing the extra spaces and
converting the strings into the same format -- that is, the same
capitalization and one space between words.

Insert the code in the loop:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{vote }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ vote.replace(}\StringTok{"  "}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{" "}\NormalTok{).capitalize()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Results will look like:

\begin{verbatim}
Red king: 4
White icicle: 1
Cherry belle: 3
Daikon: 4
...
\end{verbatim}

The code here, inserted after
\texttt{name,\ vote\ =\ line.split("\ -\ ")}, will replace two spaces
with one and capitalize the names. In the end, with this improvement,
the different variations of Red King and Cherry Belle will be merged.

\section*{String methods}\label{string-methods}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{String methods}

\markright{String methods}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{s.strip()} returns a string with whitespace removed from the
  start and end
\item
  \texttt{s.startswith(\textquotesingle{}other\textquotesingle{})} or
  \texttt{s.endswith(\textquotesingle{}other\textquotesingle{})} tests
  if the string starts or ends with the given other string
\item
  \texttt{s.replace(\textquotesingle{}old\textquotesingle{},\ \textquotesingle{}new\textquotesingle{})}
  returns a string where all occurrences of `old' have been replaced by
  `new'
\item
  \texttt{s.split(\textquotesingle{}delim\textquotesingle{})} returns a
  list of substrings separated by the given delimiter.
\item
  \texttt{s.join(list)} opposite of split(), joins the elements in the
  given list together using the string as the delimiter.
\end{itemize}

\section*{CSV Data Files}\label{csv-data-files}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{CSV Data Files}

\markright{CSV Data Files}

In the radish survey example, data are delimited by \texttt{-}. Today,
it is very common to encounter data in the CSV format. So let's take a
close look at this format and how to process CSV files with Python.

\subsection*{CSV}\label{csv}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{CSV}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  CSV, or comma-separated values

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    A common way to express structured data in text files
  \item
    Supported by spreadsheet software, e.g.~Excel
  \item
    Can be used to import to / export from relational databases
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

In \texttt{coffee.csv}:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{"Coffee","Water","Milk","Icecream"}
\NormalTok{"Espresso","No","No","No"}
\NormalTok{"Long Black","Yes","No","No"}
\NormalTok{"Flat White","No","Yes","No"}
\NormalTok{"Cappuccino","No","Yes {-} Frothy","No"}
\NormalTok{"Affogato","No","No","Yes"}
\NormalTok{...}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

In the example here, the first line is the header with the names of data
fields. Each text line after that is a data instance, where values are
separated by a comma.

\subsection*{Python CSV}\label{python-csv}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{Python CSV}

Use the Python CSV module to process:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ csv}
\NormalTok{f}\OperatorTok{=}\BuiltInTok{open}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"coffee.csv"}\NormalTok{)}
\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ row }\KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ csv.reader(f):}
    \BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(row)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

The \texttt{csv.reader()} reads a file in the CSV format and returns
each row as a list of values. The module takes care of the split of
column values so you don't need to do that again.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Read CSV and print columns:
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ csv}
\NormalTok{f }\OperatorTok{=} \BuiltInTok{open}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"airports.dat"}\NormalTok{)}
\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ row }\KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ csv.reader(f):}
    \BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(row[}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{])}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Now that the \texttt{row} variable is a list of values, you can access
each value using the index. \texttt{row{[}1{]}}, for example, returns
the value of the second column or field.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Read and print filtered data:
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ csv}
\NormalTok{f }\OperatorTok{=} \BuiltInTok{open}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"airports.dat"}\NormalTok{)}
\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ row }\KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ csv.reader(f):}
    \ControlFlowTok{if}\NormalTok{ row[}\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{==} \StringTok{"Australia"} \KeywordTok{or}\NormalTok{ row[}\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{==} \StringTok{"Russia"}\NormalTok{:}
        \BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(row[}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{])}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  DictReader and DictWriter use dictionary objects

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    With keys and values
  \item
    Read data using field names (keys)
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ csv}
\ControlFlowTok{with} \BuiltInTok{open}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}name.csv\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{) }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ csvfile:}
\NormalTok{    reader }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ csv.DictReader(csvfile)}
    \ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ row }\KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ reader:}
        \BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(row[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}first\_name\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{], row[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}last\_name\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{])}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

We noticed earlier that a CSV usually provides the header with the names
of the fields.

When there are many fields in the data, it is easier to use names
instead of a numeric index to access the corresponding value.

With DictReader and DictWriter, you can access data in the CSV using
column names in the header.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Write to CSV files:
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ csv}
 
\NormalTok{ifile  }\OperatorTok{=} \BuiltInTok{open}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}test.csv\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"rb"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{reader }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ csv.reader(ifile)}
\NormalTok{ofile  }\OperatorTok{=} \BuiltInTok{open}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}ttest.csv\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"wb"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{writer }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ csv.writer(ofile, delimiter}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, quotechar}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}"\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, quoting}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{csv.QUOTE\_ALL)}
 
\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ row }\KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ reader:}
\NormalTok{    writer.writerow(row)}
 
\NormalTok{ifile.close()}
\NormalTok{ofile.close()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Because CSV format is a plain text format that uses comma as the
delimiter, you can simply open a file and write to it using the
comma-delimited style.

To make sure data are written consistently according to the format,
especially if you have special characters such as comma in the data, you
may consider using the \texttt{csv.writer()}.

\section*{Other Formats and Tools}\label{other-formats-and-tools}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Other Formats and Tools}

\markright{Other Formats and Tools}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  JSON, JavaScript Object Notation
\item
  XML, Extensible Markup Language
\item
  YAML, YAML Ain\textquotesingle t Markup Language, a human-friendly
  data serialization format
\item
  Relational database and SQL
\end{itemize}

There are other commonly used formats such as JSON and XML, and we will
discuss them in another lecture.

\subsection*{Pandas}\label{pandas}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{Pandas}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Data loading and processing, \ldots{}
\item
  Data preprocessing, selection, \ldots{}
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ pandas }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ pd}
\NormalTok{iris\_filename }\OperatorTok{=} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}datasets{-}uci{-}iris.csv\textquotesingle{}}
\NormalTok{iris }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ pd.read\_csv(iris\_filename, sep}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{},\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, decimal}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}.\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }
\NormalTok{         header}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{None}\NormalTok{, names}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ [}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}sepal\_length\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}sepal\_width\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }
                              \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}petal\_length\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}petal\_width’,}
\ErrorTok{                              \textquotesingle{}target\textquotesingle{}]}
\NormalTok{         )}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Besides basic file read/write, Python has a wide range of tools and
packages for loading, cleaning, and preprocessing data. \texttt{Pandas},
for example, is a very powerful library for data analysis and
manipulation; along with \texttt{NumPy}, which makes it easier to
compute data in vectors and multi-dimensional arrays, and \texttt{SciPy}
for scientific computing.

\subsection*{NumPy}\label{numpy}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{NumPy}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  NumPy for data processing and operations on vectors, arrays, etc.
\end{itemize}

\subsection*{SciPy}\label{scipy}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{SciPy}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist

\end{itemize}

\section*{Recovered activity}\label{sec-lab-python-data-types}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Recovered activity}

\markright{Recovered activity}

\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced jigsaw, arc=.35mm, bottomrule=.15mm, bottomtitle=1mm, breakable, colback=white, colbacktitle=quarto-callout-note-color!10!white, colframe=quarto-callout-note-color-frame, coltitle=black, left=2mm, leftrule=.75mm, opacityback=0, opacitybacktitle=0.6, rightrule=.15mm, title=\textcolor{quarto-callout-note-color}{\faInfo}\hspace{0.5em}{Historical source and execution note}, titlerule=0mm, toprule=.15mm, toptitle=1mm]

This activity was recovered from
\texttt{python/python\_data\_types.ipynb}. Its code is preserved but is
not executed during the public book build, so readers can inspect it
without requiring legacy package versions. Download the
\href{../notebooks/python-data-types.ipynb}{source notebook} to run and

\end{tcolorbox}

\subsection*{Programming -- Data Types}\label{programming-data-types}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{Programming -- Data Types}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Basic data representation: binary digits
\item
  We need data types

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    With respect to the problem at hand
  \item
    Based on primitive data types as building blocks
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Computation is associated with data types:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Characters and strings can be sorted, concatenated
  \item
    Integers and numbers can added, subtracted, and multiplied
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

On the lower level, all data and variables in a computer are represented
with binary digits, 0 and 1s. On the application level, however, we need
different data types and ways in which data can be operated with respect
to the problems we have. We need the basic building blocks, or primitive
data types, to construct more comnplex data objects.

Ultimately, whatever computation we can perform is associated with data
types. For example, characters and strings can be sorted and
concatenated. Integers and numbers in general, on the other hand, can be
added, subtracted, and multiplied.

\section*{Data abstraction}\label{data-abstraction}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Data abstraction}

\markright{Data abstraction}

Abstract data type (ADT)

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Logical description of data and related operations on the data
\item
  Abstract from actual implementation
\item
  Actual implementation (data structure) using programming constructs
  and primitive data types
\end{itemize}

The notion of Abstract Data Type (ADT) represents a logical description
of data and related operations on the data. It is an abstraction or a
blueprint of the actual implementation, which will be based on
programming constructs and primitive data types.

\section*{Basic Python: Atomic Data
Types}\label{basic-python-atomic-data-types}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Basic Python: Atomic Data Types}

\markright{Basic Python: Atomic Data Types}

Numeric types:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{int} class for integer
\item
  \texttt{Float} class for floating point data types
\end{itemize}

Operations allowed:

\begin{verbatim}
  + - * / 
  **       exponentiation
  %        modulo
  //       integer division
\end{verbatim}

There are built-in basic atomic data types, or classes, in Python.
Numeric types such as \texttt{int} and \texttt{Float} can be computed
with operators such as division, exponentiation, modulo, among others.

\section*{Basic Python -- Atomic Data
Types}\label{basic-python-atomic-data-types-1}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Basic Python -- Atomic Data Types}

\markright{Basic Python -- Atomic Data Types}

Relational and Logical Operators:

{\def\LTcaptype{none} % do not increment counter
\begin{longtable}[]{@{}lll@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
Operation Name & Operator & Meaning \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
less than & \textless{} & \\
greater than & \textgreater{} & \\
less than or equal to & \textless= & \\
greater than or equal to & \textgreater= & \\
equal & == & \\
not equal & != & \\
logical and & and & Both true for result to be true \\
logical or & or & Either true for result to be true \\
logical not & not & Negates true to false, false to true \\
\end{longtable}
}

Numeric values are ordinal data and you can use relational operators
such as less than or greater than to evaluate and compare different
values. The result of an relational expression is either true or false,
a boolean type.

There are ways in which boolean values or boolean expressions can be
combined. Using \texttt{and}, \texttt{or}, and \texttt{not}, you will be
able to construct any complex conditions.

\section*{Basic Python -- Atomic Data
Types}\label{basic-python-atomic-data-types-2}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Basic Python -- Atomic Data Types}

\markright{Basic Python -- Atomic Data Types}

Try these numeric operator examples:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"5+3*4 ="}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{5}\OperatorTok{+}\DecValTok{3}\OperatorTok{*}\DecValTok{4}\NormalTok{)}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"20/5 ="}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{20}\OperatorTok{/}\DecValTok{5}\NormalTok{)}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"11/4 ="}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{11}\OperatorTok{/}\DecValTok{4}\NormalTok{)}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"11//4 ="}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{11}\OperatorTok{//}\DecValTok{4}\NormalTok{)}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"11\%4 ="}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{11}\OperatorTok{\%}\DecValTok{4}\NormalTok{)}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"5/20 ="}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{5}\OperatorTok{/}\DecValTok{20}\NormalTok{)}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"5//20 ="}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{5}\OperatorTok{//}\DecValTok{20}\NormalTok{)}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"2**10 ="}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{2}\OperatorTok{**}\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{)}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"100**2 ="}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{100}\OperatorTok{**}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

You can pause here and test some the operators. Change some of the
expression and run them again. Do they meet your expectation?

\section*{Basic Python -- Boolean}\label{basic-python-boolean}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Basic Python -- Boolean}

\markright{Basic Python -- Boolean}

Boolean data type: + \texttt{bool} class + True or False value

Examples:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{x }\OperatorTok{=} \VariableTok{True}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"x is"}\NormalTok{, x)}
\NormalTok{y }\OperatorTok{=} \VariableTok{False}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"y is"}\NormalTok{, y)}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"x or y is"}\NormalTok{, x }\KeywordTok{or}\NormalTok{ y)}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"x and y is"}\NormalTok{, x }\KeywordTok{and}\NormalTok{ y)}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"not (x and y) is"}\NormalTok{, }\KeywordTok{not}\NormalTok{ (x }\KeywordTok{and}\NormalTok{ y))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

You can see a boolean type is either \texttt{True} or \texttt{False}.
And you can combine them using operators such as \texttt{and},
\texttt{or}, and \texttt{not}.

Another set of examples:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Is 5 equal to 10?"}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{5} \OperatorTok{==} \DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{)}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Is 10 greater than 5?"}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{10} \OperatorTok{\textgreater{}} \DecValTok{5}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{x }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{15}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Is x between 10 and 20?"}\NormalTok{, (x}\OperatorTok{\textgreater{}=}\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{) }\KeywordTok{and}\NormalTok{ (x}\OperatorTok{\textless{}=}\DecValTok{20}\NormalTok{))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Here you see the result of an equality and inequality comparison is also
a bool value. For example, \texttt{5==10} is False, and it is True that
\texttt{10\textgreater{}5}. Again, you can combine multiple conditions
using the logical operators.

Again, change the expressions and test them for yourself. Do logical
operators such as \texttt{and}, \texttt{or}, and \texttt{not} make
sense?

\section*{Basic Python - Collections}\label{basic-python---collections}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Basic Python - Collections}

\markright{Basic Python - Collections}

Collection Data Types + Ordered collections: 1. Lists 2. Strings 3.
Tuples + Unordered collections: * Sets * Dictionaries

So far the data types are associated with single data values, such as a
number, an boolean expression.

In data processing, we often need to deal with a collection of data
values. There are multiple types of collection data. You may have an
ordered collection such as a list, a sequence of characters as a string,
and data tuples. In other situations, an unordered collection such as a
set or a dictionary might have been what you need.

Let's discuss some of these collecton data types.

\section*{Basic Python - List}\label{basic-python---list}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Basic Python - List}

\markright{Basic Python - List}

List: \textgreater{} an ordered collection of zero or more references to
data objects

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Empty list: \texttt{{[}{]}}
\item
  Items are delimited by comma
\item
  Can be heterogeneous (data objects from same or different classes /
  types)
\end{itemize}

If \texttt{x} variable is a list: + \texttt{x{[}index{]}} references the
item in the \texttt{index+1} position of the list + \texttt{x{[}0{]}}
the first item, \texttt{x{[}1{]}} the seond, and so on + You can
retrieve the value, or assign a new value using
\texttt{x{[}index{]}=...}

A list is an ordered collection of zero or more (references to) data
objects. An empty list without data is constructed by a pairs of square
brackets. By adding values to the brackets, we can add new data to the
list. Multiple values are separated by comma. In Python, a list can be
either homogeneous (i.e.~consisting of data of the same type) or
heterogeneous (i.e.~data of different types or classes). This makes
programming flexible as well as dangerous.

To access an individual element in the list, you can use an index to
refer to a specific position in the list. The index is an integer
starting from 0, which references the first element; 1 refers to the
second, and so on. You can get the value of an element, or assign a new
value to it using the assignment operator.

Examples of \texttt{list}:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{students }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ [}\StringTok{"John"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"Mary"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"Peter"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"Paul"}\NormalTok{]}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Students are: "}\NormalTok{, students)}
\NormalTok{x }\OperatorTok{=} \VariableTok{True}
\NormalTok{mix }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ [}\StringTok{"rock"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"paper"}\NormalTok{, x, }\DecValTok{5}\NormalTok{]}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"What\textquotesingle{}s in there? "}\NormalTok{, mix)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

In the examples, we can have a list of names and they are homogeneous --
every element is a string here in the students lilst. You can also have
data of different types in the list such as the \texttt{mix} variable,
which has strings as well as a bool value and a number.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{m }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ [}\DecValTok{30}\NormalTok{]}\OperatorTok{*}\DecValTok{12}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Does each month have 30 days? "}\NormalTok{, m)}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"How many days in February? "}\NormalTok{, m[}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{])}
\NormalTok{m[}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{29}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Days in February, now that it is a leap year: "}\NormalTok{, m[}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{])}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

To quickly create a list of many data values, you can simply multiply a
list by a number. Here for example, you have a one-element list
\texttt{{[}30{]}} and, when multiplied by 12, it becomes a list of 12
items with the same value.

You can then change the value of a speicific item by using the indexing
we discussed early. For example, \texttt{m{[}1{]}} references the second
item in the list, which can be used for the month of February.

Additional operations of a list: + Using predefined functions (methods)
of the list class) + For a variable that is of the list type

List method examples:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]

\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

In the example here, we fist have a list of 6 weekdays in the variable
\texttt{week}. The \texttt{week.pop()} is called, the last item is
called and \texttt{Sabbath?} is removed; and with
\texttt{week.append()}, we add Saturday to the end of the list, and
insert \texttt{Tuesday} at position 2, which will placed before the
existing \texttt{Wednesday}.

\section*{Basic Python - List
functions}\label{basic-python---list-functions}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Basic Python - List functions}

\markright{Basic Python - List functions}

List-related functions:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{range(..)} defines a sequence of values in a range (iterable)
\item
  \texttt{list(...)} constructs a range of values based on an iterable
\item
  \texttt{len(x)} returns the number of items in \texttt{x}
\end{itemize}

Examples:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{it }\OperatorTok{=} \BuiltInTok{range}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{)}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"The iterable is in"}\NormalTok{, it)}
\NormalTok{sequence }\OperatorTok{=} \BuiltInTok{list}\NormalTok{(it) }
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"A list thus generated is"}\NormalTok{, sequence)}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"which has"}\NormalTok{, }\BuiltInTok{len}\NormalTok{(sequence), }\StringTok{"values"}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\texttt{range(10)} create a range between 0 and 10, but does not include
10. A list can then be created based on the range, which is an iterable,
and the results are 10 integers from 0 to 9.

\section*{Basic Python - String}\label{basic-python---string}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Basic Python - String}

\markright{Basic Python - String}

String, a list of characters: \textgreater{} a sequential collection of
zero or more letters, numbers, and other symbols

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{season }\OperatorTok{=} \StringTok{"Spring"}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"The string has"}\NormalTok{, }\BuiltInTok{len}\NormalTok{(season), }\StringTok{"characters"}\NormalTok{)}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"The third character is"}\NormalTok{, season[}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{])}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Because a string is essentially a list, you can continue to use
functions such as \texttt{len()} for a list and the same indexing
mechanism to access individual items or characters in the string.

String methods, with a string variable \texttt{x}:

In the example, you can use the \texttt{find()} method to search the
stirng and find out where a substring such as ``P'' appears first. You
can use the \texttt{split()} method to divide the full name into first,
middle, and last name, with the space as delimiter.

\section*{Basic Python - Tuple}\label{basic-python---tuple}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Basic Python - Tuple}

\markright{Basic Python - Tuple}

Tuple \textgreater{} immutable sequence of data objects

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Similar to list
\item
  Cannot be changed (immutable) once constructed
\item
  Less overhead and more memory efficient than list
\item
  Values comma-delimited in parentheses \texttt{()}
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{slogan }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ (}\StringTok{"I"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"am"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"here"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"to"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"stay"}\NormalTok{)}
\CommentTok{\# works like a list}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\BuiltInTok{len}\NormalTok{(slogan))}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"What did you say?"}\NormalTok{, slogan[}\DecValTok{4}\NormalTok{])}
\CommentTok{\# slogan[4] = "leave"    \# Sorry, but you cannot make me leave}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

A Tuple behaves like a list. But try if you can uncomment the last
statement and see if you can change the value in a tuple. What happened?
Why?

\section*{Basic Python -- Set}\label{basic-python-set}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Basic Python -- Set}

\markright{Basic Python -- Set}

A set is \textgreater{} an unordered collection of zero or more
immutable data objects.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Every item is unique (with no duplicates)
\item
  Empty set with \texttt{set()} or \texttt{\{\}}
\item
  Values comma-delimited in curly braces \texttt{\{\}}
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{chess }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ \{}\StringTok{"King"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"Queen"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"Knight"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"Knight"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"Bishop"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"Bishop"}\NormalTok{\}}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(chess)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

So you see only the unique elements are preserved even though we enter
multiple \texttt{knights} and \texttt{bishops} in the set.

Set methods, with a variable \texttt{x} of the type:

There are also methods to add and remove elements.

\begin{Shaded}

\NormalTok{john }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ \{}\StringTok{"Python"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"Java"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"R"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"C++"}\NormalTok{\}}

\NormalTok{team }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ john.union(mary)}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Team skills"}\NormalTok{, team)}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Common skills"}\NormalTok{,john.intersection(mary))}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Mary\textquotesingle{}s unique skills"}\NormalTok{, mary.difference(john))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

With built-in methods of the \texttt{set} class, you can easily find out
-- between John and Mary -- what's in common, what's the joint skill
set, or what unique skills Mary has.

\section*{Basic Python -- Dictionary}\label{basic-python-dictionary}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Basic Python -- Dictionary}

\markright{Basic Python -- Dictionary}

A dictionary is \textgreater{} an unordered structure of associated
pairs of items:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Each pair is a key and a value
\item
  Pairs are comma delimited in curly braces
\item
  Each value can be referenced using its key in square bracket
  \texttt{{[}key{]}}
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# state population data}
\NormalTok{pop }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ \{}\StringTok{"PA"}\NormalTok{:}\DecValTok{12813969}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"NY"}\NormalTok{:}\DecValTok{19491339}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"FL"}\NormalTok{:}\DecValTok{21646155}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"TX"}\NormalTok{:}\DecValTok{29087070}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"CA"}\NormalTok{:}\DecValTok{39747267}\NormalTok{ \}}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Pennsylvannia:"}\NormalTok{, pop[}\StringTok{"PA"}\NormalTok{])}
\NormalTok{ca\_to\_pa }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ pop[}\StringTok{"CA"}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{//}\NormalTok{ pop[}\StringTok{"PA"}\NormalTok{]}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"CA population is about"}\NormalTok{, ca\_to\_pa, }\StringTok{"times that of PA."}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

A dictionary is very useful and highly efficient to keep track of data
associated with unique identifiers. The example here keeps state
population data and can quickly look it up using a two-letter, unqiue
state name.

Dictionary methods, with a variable \texttt{x}:

{\def\LTcaptype{none} % do not increment counter
\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1778}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2667}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.5556}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Method
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Use.
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright

\end{minipage} \\

\end{longtable}

More can be found at:
https://www.w3schools.com/python/python\_ref\_dictionary.asp

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{votes }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ \{}\StringTok{"John"}\NormalTok{:}\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"Mary"}\NormalTok{:}\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{\}}
\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ candidate }\KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ votes:}
    \BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(candidate, }\StringTok{"received"}\NormalTok{, votes[candidate], }\StringTok{"votes, so far. "}\NormalTok{)}

\CommentTok{\# count last three votes}
\NormalTok{votes[}\StringTok{"John"}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{+=} \DecValTok{1}
\NormalTok{votes[}\StringTok{"Mary"}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ votes[}\StringTok{"Mary"}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{+} \DecValTok{1}
\NormalTok{votes[}\StringTok{"Paul"}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ votes.get(}\StringTok{"Paul"}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{) }\OperatorTok{+} \DecValTok{1}     \CommentTok{\# if no such key, treat current value as 0}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Final votes:"}\NormalTok{, votes)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

In this example of voting counting. Every time we see a vote for one
candidate, we can add 1 to the candidate's key in the directory.
However, a potential problem may arise when a key does not exist yet.
The \texttt{get} method is useful when certain keys may not have existed
in the middle of a counting process.

Paul, for example, has not received a vote so his name is not in the
dictionary yet. So when you use \texttt{votes.get("Paul",0)}, it will
return 0 if the key is not there yet so we can add 1 to it. Without the
default value 0, the program will trigger an error when you try to add 1
to a null value.

\section*{Basic data structures}\label{basic-data-structures}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Basic data structures}

\markright{Basic data structures}

Linear structures + Stacks, queues, deques, and lists + Data collections
where * Items are ordered depending on how they are added/removed *
Position relative to the other elements that came before and after + Two
ends of linear structures * Where to add and where to remove?

In this lecture, I'd like to focus on linear data structures. Stacks,
queues and deques behave like a list, but they differ in the order of
adding new items and the positions where items can be accessed and
removed from the list. The design to allow the access of an item from
one end but not the other seems restrictive but makes it easier, and
sometimes more efficient, to translate a real world problem into a
computer solution.

\section*{Stacks}\label{stacks}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Stacks}

\markright{Stacks}

Here is a stack of books, when you put one on top of the other. There is
a natural order in which the books are placed in a stack. You start with
the one in the bottom, and add another on top, and continue that way.
When you remove it, the most natural way is to remove one book at a time
from the top.

So regardless of whether you are to add or to remove, you access the
stack from the top.

A stack, or ``push-down stack'': + An ordered collection of items where
* the addition of new items and the removal of existing items * always
takes place at the same end. + Last-in first-out (LIFO)

Because of this mechanism of placing one on top of the last and pushing
it down, a stack can be thought of as a push-down stack. It is an
ordered collection of items where the addition and removal happens at
the same end, that is, the top.

Think about this. When you try to remove a book from a stack, you first
remove the last one you put in. So a stack is last-in first-out.

\subsection*{Stack Design}\label{stack-design}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{Stack Design}

Abstract data type (class): + \texttt{Stack()} creates a new stack that
is empty. It needs no parameters and returns an empty stack. +
\texttt{push(item)} adds a new item to the top of the stack. It needs
the item and returns nothing. + \texttt{pop()} removes the top item from
the stack. It needs no parameters and returns the item. The stack is
modified. + \texttt{peek()} returns the top item from the stack but does
not remove it. It needs no parameters. The stack is not modified. +
\texttt{is\_empty()} tests to see whether the stack is empty. It needs
no parameters and returns a boolean value. + \texttt{size()} returns the
number of items on the stack. It needs no parameters and returns an
integer.

Imagine if you are to design Stack data structure, perhaps here are some
of the operations or methods you need to have: to push and add a new
item, to pop or remove the last item, and to check the size of the
stack, that is, the number of items in it.

\subsection*{Stack Implementation}\label{stack-implementation}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{Stack Implementation}

An example Stack class, based on a Python list:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{class}\NormalTok{ Stack: }
    \KeywordTok{def} \FunctionTok{\_\_init\_\_}\NormalTok{(}\VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{):}
        \VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{.items }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ []}
    
    \KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ is\_empty(}\VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{):}
        \ControlFlowTok{return} \VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{.items }\OperatorTok{==}\NormalTok{ []}
    
    \KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ push(}\VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{, item):}
        \VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{.items.append(item)}
        
    \KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ pop(}\VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{):}
        \ControlFlowTok{return} \VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{.items.pop()}
    
    \KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ peek(}\VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{):}
        \ControlFlowTok{return} \VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{.items[}\BuiltInTok{len}\NormalTok{(}\VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{.items)}\OperatorTok{{-}}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{]}
    
    \KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ size(}\VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{):}
        \ControlFlowTok{return} \BuiltInTok{len}\NormalTok{(}\VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{.items)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Now if you run this code, it will work fine but you get nothing from it.
For now, this only defines the class or the blueprint of what a Stack
is. Until you construct an actual Stack (instance) based on the
blueprint, it is not real yet.

Let's look at the Stack blueprint (class) first. The
\texttt{\_\_init\_\_} is the constructor function that will be called
when a new Stack instance is created and, at the very moment of
creation, whatever code you have here will be used for initialization.
Here, \texttt{self} refers to the new instance just created, within
which you have an \texttt{items} variable holding an empty list with the
\texttt{{[}{]}}.

The other functions implement related methods to add, remove, and access
the last (top) item in the stack, as well as those to evaluate the size
of the stack.

\subsection*{Stacks in Action}\label{stacks-in-action}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{Stacks in Action}

Now that we have the class (blueprint), let's create Stack instances
(objects) and put them into action.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{cards }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ Stack()}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Is the stack empty for now?"}\NormalTok{, cards.is\_empty())}
\NormalTok{cards.push(}\StringTok{"J"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{cards.push(}\StringTok{"Q"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{cards.push(}\StringTok{"K"}\NormalTok{)}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"The last card is"}\NormalTok{, cards.peek())}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"There are"}\NormalTok{, cards.size(), }\StringTok{"cards in the stack."}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{cards.pop()}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"We removed the top."}\NormalTok{)}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Now the last card become"}\NormalTok{, cards.peek())}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"There remain"}\NormalTok{, cards.size(), }\StringTok{"cards in the stack."}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

In the code, we first create an empty Stack \texttt{cards} by calling
the \texttt{Stack()} constructor, which executes whatever you have in
the \texttt{\_\_init\_\_} function. Next, we push or add new cards to
the \texttt{cards} stack, and when we peek, we can only access the last
one, ``King'' which is on the top. And now if we call \texttt{pop()}, we
remove the ``King''.

\subsection*{Stack Applications}\label{stack-applications}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{Stack Applications}

Why do we need Stacks? + Isn't a \texttt{list} more flexible? + Why
FILO? Isn't that restrictive and making programming more difficult?

There are very natural questions from students. But there are
applications where the restrictive design is a blessing. When the
problem you try to solve also exhibits the same restrictive
characteristics, the design will make sure related assumptions and
conditions are not violated. In fact, the proper mapping of the problem
space and the design makes coding easier.

\subsection*{Example Application}\label{example-application}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{Example Application}

Math formula checker

Correct:

\begin{verbatim}
7 - (5 + (2+3)*10)
\end{verbatim}

Incorrect:

\begin{verbatim}
7 - (5 + (2+3)*10))))
\end{verbatim}

Let's take a look at real world problems. Imagine you write a math
equation, whenever you open with \texttt{(}, you will have close it to
properly with \texttt{)} at some point. The whole equation should be
balanced, e.g.~two opening \texttt{(} matched with two closing
\texttt{)} just like the first equation above. The second equation is
incorrect because there are more closing \texttt{)} than the number of
opened ones.

Related problems in HTML markup:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\DataTypeTok{\textless{}}\KeywordTok{html}\DataTypeTok{\textgreater{}}
    \DataTypeTok{\textless{}}\KeywordTok{head}\DataTypeTok{\textgreater{}}
        \DataTypeTok{\textless{}}\KeywordTok{title}\DataTypeTok{\textgreater{}}\NormalTok{Page Title}\DataTypeTok{\textless{}/}\KeywordTok{title}\DataTypeTok{\textgreater{}}
    \DataTypeTok{\textless{}/}\KeywordTok{head}\DataTypeTok{\textgreater{}}
    \DataTypeTok{\textless{}}\KeywordTok{body}\DataTypeTok{\textgreater{}}
\NormalTok{        Page Content}
    \DataTypeTok{\textless{}/}\KeywordTok{body}\DataTypeTok{\textgreater{}}
\DataTypeTok{\textless{}/}\KeywordTok{html}\DataTypeTok{\textgreater{}}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Checking math equations is similar to checking code in programs and
markup languages. In HTML -- XML and XHTML in particular -- tags appear
in pairs. A regular HTML tag consists of an opening
\texttt{\textless{}tag\textgreater{}} and a closing one
\texttt{\textless{}/tag\textgreater{}}. They should be likewise
balanced. So how can we check them, using the idea of a stack?

So let's try to create a piece of code to check the balance of a match
equation.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# Math checker}
\NormalTok{m }\OperatorTok{=} \StringTok{"7 {-} (5 + (2+3)*10)"}
\NormalTok{s }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ Stack()}
\NormalTok{balanced }\OperatorTok{=} \VariableTok{True}
\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ symbol }\KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ m: }
    \ControlFlowTok{if}\NormalTok{ symbol }\OperatorTok{==} \StringTok{"("}\NormalTok{:}
\NormalTok{        s.push(symbol)}
    \ControlFlowTok{elif}\NormalTok{ symbol }\OperatorTok{==} \StringTok{")"}\NormalTok{:}
        \ControlFlowTok{if}\NormalTok{ s.is\_empty():}
\NormalTok{            balanced }\OperatorTok{=} \VariableTok{False}
        \ControlFlowTok{else}\NormalTok{:}
\NormalTok{            s.pop()}

\ControlFlowTok{if} \KeywordTok{not}\NormalTok{ s.is\_empty():}
\NormalTok{    balanced }\OperatorTok{=} \VariableTok{False}

\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Is it balanced? "}\NormalTok{, balanced)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

For now, we hard code the math equation to show the basic idea. You can
certainly make this a function or even a method of a class so you can
reuse it.

We create an empty Stack \texttt{s} first and assume the result to be
correct with \texttt{balanced\ =\ True}, unless we find something wrong.

Now we take each \texttt{symbol} in the equation \texttt{m} from left to
right, which is what the loop does. For each symbol:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  if it opens with a \texttt{(}, we push it to the stack.
\item
  if it closes with a \texttt{)}:
\end{enumerate}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  if stack is empty, the equation is problematic as no opening
  \texttt{(} has preceeded it;
\item
  if stack not empty, we remove (pop) the last opening \texttt{(} on top
  as it has been \textbf{balanced out} (good).
\end{itemize}

So going through the math here 7 - (5 + (2+3)*10), from left to right:

\end{Highlighting}

Two \texttt{((} remain the stack after the loop.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ControlFlowTok{if} \KeywordTok{not}\NormalTok{ s.is\_empty:}
\NormalTok{    balanced }\OperatorTok{=} \VariableTok{False}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

With extra opening \texttt{(} early in the equation, the stack will not
be balanced out (empty) after the loop. So in the end, the result will
be \texttt{False}. See code at the end:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ControlFlowTok{if} \KeywordTok{not}\NormalTok{ s.is\_empty:}
\NormalTok{    balanced }\OperatorTok{=} \VariableTok{False}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Now you may go back to the math checker code, change the formula, and
re-run the program. Does it work as you expected? Is the concept of
Stack sound like a good idea for the problem here? Thhink about HTML
code, do you think it is similar to develop a program to check whether
HTML tags are opened and closed properly?

\section*{Queue}\label{queue}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Queue}

\markright{Queue}

I suppose you don't like waiting, especially if you are late and there
is already a long line. It might be tempting to push in, but hey, you
are not supposed to do that.

A queue: + An ordered collection of items where * the addition of new
items happens at one end (rear) * the removal of existing items occurs
at the other (front) + Queues are \textbf{first-in first-out} (FIFO), or
first come first served

A waiting line is a queue, and the rule is first come first served.

A queue is defined as an ordered collection of items where the addition
of new items and the removal of existing items occur at the different
ends. This is quite the opposite of a Stack, where you access from the
same end.

So queues are first-in first-out, os first come first served.

\subsection*{Queue Design}\label{queue-design}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{Queue Design}

Queue abstract data type:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{Queue()} creates a new queue that is empty. It needs no
  parameters and returns an empty queue.
\item
  \texttt{enqueue(item)} adds a new item to the rear of the queue. It
  needs the item and returns nothing.
\item
  \texttt{dequeue()} removes the front item from the queue. It needs no
  parameters and returns the item. The queue is modified.
\item
  \texttt{is\_empty()} tests to see whether the queue is empty. It needs
  no parameters and returns a boolean value.
\item
  \texttt{size()} returns the number of items in the queue. It needs no
  parameters and returns an integer.
\end{itemize}

Again, imgine you are designing a data structure to solve a problem
related to queues. Here are potential operations you need to include to
support the construction of related algorithms, for example: enqueue to
add a new item to the end, dequeue to remove one from the front, and
methods to check the size of the queue.

\subsection*{Queue Implementation}\label{queue-implementation}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{Queue Implementation}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{class}\NormalTok{ Queue:}
    \KeywordTok{def} \FunctionTok{\_\_init\_\_}\NormalTok{(}\VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{):}
        \VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{.items }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ []}
        
    \KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ is\_empty(}\VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{):}
        \ControlFlowTok{return} \VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{.items }\OperatorTok{==}\NormalTok{ []}
    
    \KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ enqueue(}\VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{, item):}
        \VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{.items.insert(}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, item)}
    
    \KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ dequeue(}\VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{):}
        \ControlFlowTok{return} \VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{.items.pop()}
    
    \KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ size(}\VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{):}
        \ControlFlowTok{return} \BuiltInTok{len}\NormalTok{(}\VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{.items)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

The Queue class is similar to the Stack class we implemented earlier,
both using a \texttt{list} to store items. The \texttt{dequeue()} method
is similar to the \texttt{pop()} in the Stack class, which removes an
item at the end of a list.

Here, conceptually, the end of the items list is in fact the
\textbf{front} of the queue. Conversely, the \texttt{enqueue()} method
adds an item to position 0, which is, in this case, the \textbf{rear} of
the queue.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{orders }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ Queue()      }\CommentTok{\# An empty queue first}
\NormalTok{orders.enqueue(}\StringTok{"Coke"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{orders.enqueue(}\StringTok{"Burger"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{orders.enqueue(}\StringTok{"Fries"}\NormalTok{)}

\NormalTok{item }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ orders.dequeue()    }\CommentTok{\# Coke is served and dequeued}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Served"}\NormalTok{, item)}
\NormalTok{item }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ orders.dequeue()    }\CommentTok{\# Next, Burger is served}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Served"}\NormalTok{, item)}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"How many orders remain?"}\NormalTok{, orders.size())}
\NormalTok{item }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ orders.dequeue()}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Served"}\NormalTok{, item)}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Now, has everyone been served?"}\NormalTok{, orders.is\_empty())}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Queue has a wide range of applications, from business transactions, to
printer jobs, to any processes where things get done on a first-come
first-served basis. The example here shows a sequence of actions to
place (add) orders and to get them processed and served (removed).

\section*{Deques}\label{deques}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Deques}

\markright{Deques}

A deque, or double-ended queue + An ordered collection of items similar
to the queue * Two ends, front and rear * Items can be added and removed
from either front and rear + A deque has all capabilities of a stack and
a queue + No longer restricted to LIFO and FIFO, with access from both
ends + Consistent use of addition and removal in the context of
application

Deque is an extension of the queue structure.

Deque, or a double-ended queue, is an ordered collection of items with
two ends, the front and the rear, and items can be added and removed
from both ends. In this design, a deque has all capabilities of a stack
as well as those of a queue. It is no longer last-in first-out or
first-in first-out.

If deque sounds a like weird idea, think about a train. You can add or
remove cars to the end of a train; likewise, you attach them to the
front. An engine can be attached to either side depending on which
direction you want to go.

\subsection*{Deque Design}\label{deque-design}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{Deque Design}

Deque abstract data type: + \texttt{Deque()} creates a new deque that is
empty. It needs no parameters and returns an empty deque. +
\texttt{add\_front(item)} adds a new item to the front of the deque. It
needs the item and returns nothing. + \texttt{add\_rear(item)} adds a
new item to the rear of the deque. It needs the item and returns
nothing. + \texttt{remove\_front()} removes the front item from the
deque. It needs no parameters and returns the item. The deque is
modified. + \texttt{remove\_rear()} removes the rear item from the
deque. It needs no parameters and returns the item. The deque is
modified. + \texttt{is\_empty()} tests to see whether the deque is
empty. It needs no parameters and returns a boolean value. +
\texttt{size()} returns the number of items in the deque. It needs no
parameters and returns an integer.

When designing a deque type, you need to have methods to add and remove
from both ends: add\_front(), add\_rear(), remove\_front(), and
remove\_rear().

\subsection*{Deque class
implementation}\label{deque-class-implementation}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{Deque class implementation}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{class}\NormalTok{ Deque: }
    \KeywordTok{def} \FunctionTok{\_\_init\_\_}\NormalTok{(}\VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{):}
        \VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{.items }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ []}
    
    \KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ is\_empty(}\VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{):}
        \ControlFlowTok{return} \VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{.items }\OperatorTok{==}\NormalTok{ []}
    
    \KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ add\_front(}\VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{, item): }
        \VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{.items.append(item)}
    
    \KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ add\_rear(}\VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{, item):}
        \VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{.items.insert(}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, item)}
        
    \KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ remove\_front(}\VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{):}
        \ControlFlowTok{return} \VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{.items.pop()}
    
    \KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ remove\_rear(}\VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{):}
        \ControlFlowTok{return} \VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{.items.pop(}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{)}
    
    \KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ size(}\VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{):}
        \ControlFlowTok{return} \BuiltInTok{len}\NormalTok{(}\VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{.items)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Compare this Deque class code to the Queue and Stack we had earlier. It
is a combination of methods from the two classes, in different names
though.

\subsection*{Deque in Action}\label{deque-in-action}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{Deque in Action}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{train\_cars }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ Deque()}

\CommentTok{\# attach the cars in the end}
\NormalTok{train\_cars.add\_rear(}\StringTok{"Passengers"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{train\_cars.add\_rear(}\StringTok{"Dining"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{train\_cars.remove\_rear()}
\NormalTok{train\_cars.add\_rear(}\StringTok{"Parlor"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{train\_cars.add\_rear(}\StringTok{"Dining"}\NormalTok{)}

\CommentTok{\# attach caboose}
\NormalTok{train\_cars.add\_rear(}\StringTok{"Caboose"}\NormalTok{)}

\CommentTok{\# put engine in front}
\NormalTok{train\_cars.add\_front(}\StringTok{"Engine"}\NormalTok{)}

\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"How long is the train?"}\NormalTok{, train\_cars.size(), }\StringTok{"cars"}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Imagine you are to arrange train engine with different cars. You may
play with the code to see the different orders in which the same
sequence of cars can be constructed.

\section*{Objectives}\label{objectives}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Objectives}

\markright{Objectives}

Please solve the following problem with Python programming using proper
data structures.

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Create a \textbf{Jupyter Notebook} with code and markdown
  descriptions;
\item
  \textbf{Test your code} and make sure it works properly;
\item
  Submit your Jupyter Notebook (.ipynb) to \textbf{blackboard}.
\end{enumerate}

A similar application to the parentheses matching problem is HTML
(hypertext markup language) validation. In HTML, tags exist in both
opening and closing forms and must be balanced to properly describe a
web document.

This simple HTML code (string) here:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\DataTypeTok{\textless{}}\KeywordTok{html}\DataTypeTok{\textgreater{}}
\DataTypeTok{\textless{}}\KeywordTok{head}\DataTypeTok{\textgreater{}}
\DataTypeTok{\textless{}}\KeywordTok{title}\DataTypeTok{\textgreater{}}
\NormalTok{Example\textgreater{}}
\DataTypeTok{\textless{}/}\KeywordTok{title}\DataTypeTok{\textgreater{}}
\DataTypeTok{\textless{}/}\KeywordTok{head}\DataTypeTok{\textgreater{}}
\DataTypeTok{\textless{}}\KeywordTok{body}\DataTypeTok{\textgreater{}}
\DataTypeTok{\textless{}}\KeywordTok{h1}\DataTypeTok{\textgreater{}}
\NormalTok{Hello, world}
\DataTypeTok{\textless{}/}\KeywordTok{h1}\DataTypeTok{\textgreater{}}
\DataTypeTok{\textless{}/}\KeywordTok{body}\DataTypeTok{\textgreater{}}
\DataTypeTok{\textless{}/}\KeywordTok{html}\DataTypeTok{\textgreater{}}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

shows the matching and nesting structure for tags in the language.

Please create a Jupyter notebook and write a Python program that can
\textbf{validate HTML code} with proper opening and åclosing tags. To
simplify the problem, we suppose the opening and closing tags are
provided in \textbf{separate lines without indentation} (as shown in the
above example).

Consult the example on the math checker (parentheses matching) in
lecture slides/notes:

python-data-structures.qmd\#sec-lab-python-data-structures
