\section*{1. Create a Stack abstract data type (2
points)}\label{create-a-stack-abstract-data-type-2-points}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{1. Create a Stack abstract data type (2
points)}

\markright{1. Create a Stack abstract data type (2 points)}

The \texttt{Stack} class should include the following methods:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{push(item)} function to add a new item to the stack
\item
  \texttt{pop()} function to remove and \textbf{return} the top item
  from the stack
\item
  \texttt{peek()} to return the top item in the stack
\item
  \texttt{is\_empty()} to test whether the stack is empty
\item
  \texttt{size()} to tell the size (number of items) in the stack
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{class}\NormalTok{ Stack: }
    \CommentTok{"""}
\CommentTok{    The Stack class to be implemented. }
\CommentTok{    Please define all required methods }
\CommentTok{    within the class structure. }
\CommentTok{    """}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\section*{2. Create a check\_html function (5
points)}\label{create-a-check_html-function-5-points}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{2. Create a check\_html function (5
points)}

\markright{2. Create a check\_html function (5 points)}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ check\_html(html\_string):}
    \CommentTok{"""Validates an HTML string (text)}
\CommentTok{    Arguments: }
\CommentTok{        html\_string (string): the HTML string with tags in each line (separated by }\CharTok{\textbackslash{}n}\CommentTok{)}
\CommentTok{        }
\CommentTok{    Returns: }
\CommentTok{        bool: True if the HTML tags are balanced; False if otherwise. }
\CommentTok{    """}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Here is what the function should do:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Create a \texttt{balanced} variable and set its initial value to
  \texttt{True}
\item
  Create a new Stack object;
\item
  Use the split() method of a string to split it into text lines;
\item
  Create a loop to read each text line:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    You can use a for loop such as --
  \end{itemize}

\begin{verbatim}
 ```python
 for line in text_lines:
 ```
\end{verbatim}

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    If the text line matches an opening tag
    (e.g.~\texttt{\textless{}h1\textgreater{}}), add the tag name
    (e.g.~``h1'') to the stack;

    \begin{itemize}
    \item
      You can use expressions such as \texttt{"\textless{}"\ in\ line}
      to check if the symbol ``\textless{}'' appears in the string
      variable
    \item
      You should make sure ``\textless{}'' is in the text line AND ``/''
      is NOT in it for an openning tag;
    \item
      Use the logical \texttt{and} operator to connect multiple logical
      conditions;
    \item
      Use statements such as
      \texttt{tag\_name\ =\ line.replace("\textless{}","")} to remove
      special symbols, so you can reduce it to its tag name only;
    \end{itemize}
  \item
    If the text line is a closing tag
    (e.g.~\texttt{\textless{}/h1\textgreater{}}), check whether the
    stack is empty:

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      The HTML code is unbalanced if stack is currently empty:

      \begin{itemize}
      \tightlist
      \item
        Please use the \texttt{is\_empty()} method of the stack object.
      \end{itemize}
    \item
      If not empty, use the stack object's \texttt{pop()} to read and
      remove the top (last) item from stack:

      \begin{itemize}
      \item
        Again, use the \texttt{replace()} method of the text line string
        variable to remove symbols \texttt{\textless{}/\textgreater{}},
        to reduce it to its tag name only;
      \item
        If the top (last) tag matches (\texttt{==}) the current closing
        tag, then the HTML code is balanced (True);
      \item
        Otherwise, the HTML code is unbalanced (False).
      \end{itemize}
    \end{itemize}
  \item
    If the text line is ordinary content without a HTML tag, skip the
    line;

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      You do not actually need to do anything in this case.
    \end{itemize}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  After the loop, check:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    If the Stack is empty (balanced out), return the value of the
    \texttt{balanced} variable
  \item
    Otherwise, \texttt{return\ False}
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\section*{3. Function Calls (0.5 point)}\label{function-calls-0.5-point}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{3. Function Calls (0.5 point)}

\markright{3. Function Calls (0.5 point)}

Create two string variables, one with \textbf{\emph{balanced HTML}} code
and the other with \textbf{\emph{unbalanced HTML code}}.

Call the html\_checker() function \textbf{twice} in your code, each time
with each of the two variables as argument to test the program. For
example:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{str1 }\OperatorTok{=} \StringTok{"(Valid/balanced HTML code here)"}
\NormalTok{str2 }\OperatorTok{=} \StringTok{"(Invalid/unbalanced HTML code here)"}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Is the first string valid?"}\NormalTok{, check\_html(str1))}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Is the second string valid?"}\NormalTok{, check\_html(str2))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

You can use the syntax in the box here to create a string with multiple
lines.

\section*{1. Basic Statistics (2
points)}\label{basic-statistics-2-points}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{1. Basic Statistics (2 points)}

\markright{1. Basic Statistics (2 points)}

Suppose that the data for analysis includes the attribute age. The age
values for the data tuples are (in increasing order):

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{age }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ [}\DecValTok{13}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{15}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{16}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{16}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{19}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{20}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{20}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{21}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{22}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{22}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{25}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{25}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{25}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{25}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{30}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{33}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{33}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{35}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{35}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{35}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{35}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{36}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{40}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{45}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{46}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{52}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{70}\NormalTok{]}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{(\alph{enumi})}
\tightlist
\item
  What is the mean of the data? What is the median?
\end{enumerate}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# Use Numpy\textquotesingle{}s np.mean(age)}
\CommentTok{\# Use Numpy\textquotesingle{}s np.median(age)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{(\alph{enumi})}
\setcounter{enumi}{1}
\tightlist
\item
  What is the mode of the data?
\end{enumerate}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# Use Scipy\textquotesingle{}s stats.mode(age)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{(\alph{enumi})}
\setcounter{enumi}{2}
\tightlist
\item
  Plot the distribution and comment on the data's modality (i.e.,
  bimodal, trimodal, etc.)
\end{enumerate}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# Use Matplotlib\textquotesingle{}s plt.hist(age)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{(\alph{enumi})}
\setcounter{enumi}{3}
\tightlist
\item
  Can you find (roughly) the first quartile (Q1) and the third quartile
  (Q3) of the data?
\end{enumerate}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# Try: np.percentile(age, [25, 75])}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{(\alph{enumi})}
\setcounter{enumi}{4}
\item
  Give the five-number summary of the data.
\item
  Show a boxplot of the data.
\end{enumerate}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# Try: plt.boxplot(age)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{(\alph{enumi})}
\setcounter{enumi}{6}
\tightlist
\item
  What does the boxplot tell? Be thorough.
\end{enumerate}

\section*{2. IRIS Data Statistics}\label{iris-data-statistics}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{2. IRIS Data Statistics}

\markright{2. IRIS Data Statistics}

Consult IRIS data documentation online about the dataset:

http://archive.ics.uci.edu/ml/datasets/Iris

which has been included in related packages such as Sklearn and Seaborn.

Here is an example to load data into a data frame.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{iris }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ load\_iris()}
\NormalTok{iris\_df }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ pd.DataFrame(data}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.c\_[iris[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}data\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{], iris[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}target\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{]], columns}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ iris[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}feature\_names\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{+}\NormalTok{ [}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}target\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{])}
\NormalTok{iris\_df.head()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\subsection*{2.1. Attribute Types (1
point)}\label{attribute-types-1-point}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{2.1. Attribute Types (1 point)}

Examine data values in the data frame and create a table to list all
attribute data types:

{\def\LTcaptype{none} % do not increment counter
\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 10\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1143}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 10\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1238}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 10\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1714}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 10\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1810}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 10\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1905}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 10\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2190}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Attribute
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
\# Values
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Order?
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Interval?
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright

\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright

\end{minipage} \\

Sepal Length & \# & Yes / No & Yes / No & Yes / No & Nominal
/Ordinal/Interval/Ratio \\

\end{longtable}
}

\subsection*{2.2. Data Distributions (1.5
points)}\label{data-distributions-1.5-points}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{2.2. Data Distributions (1.5 points)}

Examine data distributions for each \textbf{numeric} attribute, using a
histogram with a density plot.

For example, you can use the Seaborn's \texttt{distplot()} function to
create a histogram:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{sns.distplot(iris\_df[}\StringTok{"sepal length (cm)"}\NormalTok{])}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\subsubsection*{Questions}\label{questions}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsubsection}{Questions}

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Does it look like a normal distribution?
\item
  What modality is the distribution? (\# modes)
\item
  Is the distribution skewed? If so, positively or negatively?
\end{enumerate}

\subsubsection*{Repeat for Each
Attribute}\label{repeat-for-each-attribute}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsubsection}{Repeat for Each Attribute}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Repeat the above for each numeric attribute, and answer the questions.
\item
  Pick a distribution that is unlikely a normal distribution
\item
  Generate a QQ plot on the actual quantiles vs.~normal distribution
  quantiles
\item
  Is it a normal distribution according to QQ? Why or why not?
\end{itemize}

Example code to generate a QQ plot against normal:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{probplot(iris\_df[}\StringTok{"sepal length (cm)"}\NormalTok{], dist}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"norm"}\NormalTok{, plot}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{plt)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\subsection*{2.3. Subset Data Distributions (2
points)}\label{subset-data-distributions-2-points}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{2.3. Subset Data Distributions (2
points)}

Now take a data subset for a specific \texttt{target} (species). For
example:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{iris\_df[iris\_df[}\StringTok{"target"}\NormalTok{]}\OperatorTok{==}\FloatTok{0.0}\NormalTok{]}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

will limit the data to rows with target value \texttt{0.0}.

Produce and exmamine each numeric attribute's data distribution for each
target.

Example code to plot subset distribution:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{sns.distplot(iris\_df[iris\_df[}\StringTok{"target"}\NormalTok{]}\OperatorTok{==}\FloatTok{0.0}\NormalTok{][}\StringTok{"sepal length (cm)"}\NormalTok{])}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\subsubsection*{Questions}\label{questions-1}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsubsection}{Questions}

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Does it look like a normal distribution?
\item
  What modality is the distribution? (\# modes)
\item
  Is the distribution symmetric or skewed? If so, positively or
  negatively?
\end{enumerate}

\subsubsection*{Repeat for Each Target}\label{repeat-for-each-target}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsubsection}{Repeat for Each Target}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Repeat the above for \textbf{each numeric attribute} and \textbf{each
  target} level (0, 1, 2).
\item
  Pick a distribution that looks like a normal distribution.
\item
  Generate a QQ plot on the actual quantiles vs.~normal distribution
  quantiles
\item
  Is it a normal distribution according to QQ? Why or why not?
\end{itemize}

Example code to generate a QQ plot against the normal distribution:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{probplot(iris\_df[iris\_df[}\StringTok{"target"}\NormalTok{]}\OperatorTok{==}\FloatTok{1.0}\NormalTok{][}\StringTok{"petal length (cm)"}\NormalTok{], dist}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"norm"}\NormalTok{, plot}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{plt)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\subsection*{2.4. Boxplots vs.~Target Levels (1.5
points)}\label{boxplots-vs.-target-levels-1.5-points}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{2.4. Boxplots vs.~Target Levels (1.5
points)}

For each numeric attribute, generate boxplots across the target levels.

For example:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{sns.boxplot(x}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"target"}\NormalTok{, y}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"sepal length (cm)"}\NormalTok{, data}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{iris\_df)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\subsubsection*{For Each Attribute}\label{for-each-attribute}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsubsection}{For Each Attribute}

Examine the boxplots and discuss the following questions:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Do the distributions show a \textbf{constant variance} on the
  different target levels?
\item
  On each level, does it show a \textbf{symmetric} distribution? Or is
  it positively or negatively skewed?
\item
  Are the means constant or different? What does it mean (about
  potential correlation)?
\end{enumerate}

Please do the boxplots and answer the question for each numeric
attribute.

\section*{3. Correlation, Similarity and
Distance}\label{correlation-similarity-and-distance}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{3. Correlation, Similarity and Distance}

\markright{3. Correlation, Similarity and Distance}

\subsection*{3.1. Scatter Plots (0.25
point)}\label{scatter-plots-0.25-point}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{3.1. Scatter Plots (0.25 point)}

Reload the IRIS data from Seaborn and create pairwise scatter plots for
IRIS variables:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{iris }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ sns.load\_dataset(}\StringTok{"iris"}\NormalTok{)   }\CommentTok{\# reload data from seaborn}
\NormalTok{sns.pairplot(iris)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Optional: You may also consider using the hue parameter to color code
the target (species) on the plots:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{sns.pairplot(iris, hue}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"species"}\NormalTok{)}\OperatorTok{;}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\subsection*{3.2. Visual Examination (1
point)}\label{visual-examination-1-point}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{3.2. Visual Examination (1 point)}

Examine the scatterplots and identify \textbf{two pairs} of numeric
attributes: 1. One pair of \textbf{strongly correlated} attributes;
Briefly explain \textbf{why or whether} they are positively or
negatively correlated? 2. One pair of weakly correlated or
\textbf{uncorrelated} attributes; Briefly explain \textbf{why} they
appear to be uncorrelated.

\subsection*{3.3. Pearson Correlation (1
point)}\label{pearson-correlation-1-point}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{3.3. Pearson Correlation (1 point)}

Now compute Pearson correlation on the data attributes.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{pcorr }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ iris.corr(method}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}pearson\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{pcorr}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

For the two pairs of attributes you identified earlier, single out their
Pearson correlation scores:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Strongly correlated pair: Pearson score
\item
  Uncorrelated pair: Pearson score
\end{enumerate}

Note: please keep \textbf{3 digits} after the decimal point,
e.g.~\emph{0.123}.

What do the scores tell you? Are they consistent with your visual
observation?

\subsection*{3.4. Euclidean Distance (0.25
point)}\label{euclidean-distance-0.25-point}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{3.4. Euclidean Distance (0.25 point)}

Compute Euclidean distances for the two pairs of attributes. For
example:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{a }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ iris[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}sepal\_length\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{]}
\NormalTok{b }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ iris[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}sepal\_width\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{]}
\NormalTok{d }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ linalg.norm(a}\OperatorTok{{-}}\NormalTok{b)}
\NormalTok{d}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\subsection*{3.5. Cosine 1 with Original Values (0.25
point)}\label{cosine-1-with-original-values-0.25-point}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{3.5. Cosine 1 with Original Values
(0.25 point)}

Compute Cosine similarities for the two pairs of attributes. For
example:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{a }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ iris[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}petal\_length\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{]}
\NormalTok{b }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ iris[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}petal\_width\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{]}
\NormalTok{c1 }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.dot(a,b)}\OperatorTok{/}\NormalTok{(linalg.norm(a)}\OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{linalg.norm(b))}
\NormalTok{c1}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\subsection*{3.6. Cosine 2 with Vectors from Mean (0.25
point)}\label{cosine-2-with-vectors-from-mean-0.25-point}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{3.6. Cosine 2 with Vectors from Mean
(0.25 point)}

Subtract the mean from each attribute value and make each attribute
(column) a vector from its mean (instead of from the origin), and
compute Cosine similarities. For example:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{a }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ iris[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}petal\_length\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{]}
\NormalTok{a2 }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ a }\OperatorTok{{-}}\NormalTok{ np.mean(a)}
\NormalTok{b }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ iris[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}petal\_width\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{]}
\NormalTok{b2 }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ b }\OperatorTok{{-}}\NormalTok{ np.mean(b)}
\NormalTok{c2 }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.dot(a2,b2)}\OperatorTok{/}\NormalTok{(linalg.norm(a2)}\OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{linalg.norm(b2))}
\NormalTok{c2}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\subsection*{3.7. Summary and Observation (1
point)}\label{summary-and-observation-1-point}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{3.7. Summary and Observation (1
point)}

Compile all scores (pearson, euclidean distance, cosine 1 and cosine 2)
together as one table below:

{\def\LTcaptype{none} % do not increment counter
\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 8\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3878}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 8\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1327}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 8\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2041}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 8\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1327}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 8\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1429}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Pair
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Pearson
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Euclidean Dist
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Cosine 1
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Cosine 2
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
Strong correlation: attr 1 vs.~attr 2 & & & & \\
Weak correlation: attr 3 vs.~attr 4 & & & & \\
\end{longtable}
}

Examine the scores for each pair in the table, and answer the questions:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Does a stronger Pearson correlation necessarily lead to a smaller or
  greater Euclidean distance?
\item
  Does a strong Pearson correlation lead to a greater Cosine 1 (vectors
  from the origin)?
\item
  What do you observe is the relation between Pearson correlation and
  Cosine 2 (vectors from the mean)?
\end{enumerate}

\section*{Recovered activity}\label{sec-lab-probability-linearity}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Recovered activity}

\markright{Recovered activity}

\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced jigsaw, arc=.35mm, bottomrule=.15mm, bottomtitle=1mm, breakable, colback=white, colbacktitle=quarto-callout-note-color!10!white, colframe=quarto-callout-note-color-frame, coltitle=black, left=2mm, leftrule=.75mm, opacityback=0, opacitybacktitle=0.6, rightrule=.15mm, title=\textcolor{quarto-callout-note-color}{\faInfo}\hspace{0.5em}{Historical source and execution note}, titlerule=0mm, toprule=.15mm, toptitle=1mm]

This activity was recovered from \texttt{demo/data\_prob\_linear.ipynb}.
Its code is preserved but is not executed during the public book build,
so readers can inspect it without requiring legacy package versions.
Download the \href{../notebooks/probability-linearity.ipynb}{source
notebook} to run and modernize it interactively.

\end{tcolorbox}

Import necessary libraries in Python:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\OperatorTok{\%}\NormalTok{matplotlib inline}
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ math}
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ matplotlib.pyplot }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ plt}
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ matplotlib }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ animation, rc}
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ numpy }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ np}
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ pandas }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ pd}
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ seaborn }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ sns}
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ numpy }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ linalg}
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ scipy }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ stats}
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ IPython.display }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ HTML, display}

\NormalTok{plt.rcdefaults()}
\CommentTok{\# plt.xkcd(scale=1, length=300, randomness=5)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\section*{A. Probabilities in
Language}\label{a.-probabilities-in-language}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{A. Probabilities in Language}

\markright{A. Probabilities in Language}

Let's look at a collection of emails, with spams and hams:
https://www.kaggle.com/uciml/sms-spam-collection-dataset

Here is a local copy of the data: \href{../data/spam.csv}{spam.csv}.

\subsection*{A.1. Load Data}\label{a.1.-load-data}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{A.1. Load Data}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{emails }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ pd.read\_csv(}\StringTok{"../data/spam.csv"}\NormalTok{, encoding}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"iso8859{-}1"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{emails[[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}v1\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}v2\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{]].head()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\subsection*{A.2. Zipf's Law}\label{a.2.-zipfs-law}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{A.2. Zipf's Law}

Let's tokenize the emails and count word frequencies.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ sklearn.feature\_extraction.text }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ CountVectorizer}

\CommentTok{\# instantiate a count vectorizer}
\NormalTok{vect }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ CountVectorizer(analyzer}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"word"}\NormalTok{)}
\CommentTok{\# obtain vocabulary dictionary and return doc{-}term matrix}
\NormalTok{X }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ vect.fit\_transform(emails.v2) }
\CommentTok{\# get the identified terms}
\NormalTok{terms }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ vect.get\_feature\_names()}
\CommentTok{\# sum terms\textquotesingle{} frequencies in the entire collection}
\NormalTok{freqs }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ X.toarray().}\BuiltInTok{sum}\NormalTok{(axis}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{terms }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.array(terms)}
\NormalTok{freqs }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.array(freqs)}

\CommentTok{\# organize the tersm and frequencies in t and f columns}
\NormalTok{tfs }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ pd.DataFrame(\{}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}t\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{:terms, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}f\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{:freqs\}, columns}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}t\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}f\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{])}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Rank the terms by their frequencies:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{tfs }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ tfs.sort\_values(by}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}f\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{], ascending}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{False}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{tfs }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ tfs.reset\_index(drop}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{tfs.head()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Add the rank \texttt{r} column to start with 1:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{tfs[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}k\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ tfs.index }\OperatorTok{+} \DecValTok{1}
\NormalTok{tfs[[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}t\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}k\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}f\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{]].head()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Plot the frequency \(f_t\) vs.~rank \(k_t\):

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{plt.plot(tfs[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}k\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{], tfs[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}f\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{])}
\NormalTok{plt.xlabel(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Rank $k\_t$\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.ylabel(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Frequency $f\_t$\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.show()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Transform X and Y to log-log coordinates:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{plt.loglog(tfs[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}k\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{], tfs[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}f\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{])}
\NormalTok{plt.xlabel(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Rank $k\_t$\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.ylabel(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Frequency $f\_t$\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.show()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Now each term's probability to occur can be estimated by:

\[ p_t = \frac{f_t}{\sum_t f_t} \]

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{ttf }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ tfs[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}f\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{].}\BuiltInTok{sum}\NormalTok{()}
\NormalTok{tfs[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}p\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ tfs[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}f\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{/}\NormalTok{ ttf}
\NormalTok{tfs[[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}t\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}k\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}p\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{]].head()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Plot probability \(p_t\) vs.~rank \(k_t\):

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{plt.loglog(tfs[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}k\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{], tfs[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}p\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{])}
\NormalTok{plt.xlabel(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Rank $k\_t$\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.ylabel(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Probability $p\_t$\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.show()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Does it look like a straight line on log-log coordinates? Does it follow
the Zipf's law?

Let's perform a linear regression on the log-transformed \(p_t\) and
\(k_t\):

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ sklearn.linear\_model }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ LinearRegression}

\CommentTok{\# log transformation of k and p values}
\NormalTok{x }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ tfs[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}k\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{].values.reshape(}\OperatorTok{{-}}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{xlog }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.log(x)}
\NormalTok{y }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ tfs[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}p\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{].values.reshape(}\OperatorTok{{-}}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{ylog }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.log(y)}

\CommentTok{\# perform linear regression on log values}
\NormalTok{lm }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ LinearRegression()}
\NormalTok{lm.fit(xlog,ylog)}
\NormalTok{yplog }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ lm.predict(xlog)}

\CommentTok{\# plot the fitted (predicted) line }
\CommentTok{\# along with actual k\_t and p\_t data}
\NormalTok{plt.loglog(tfs[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}k\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{], tfs[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}p\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{], label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"Observed Data"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.loglog(x, np.exp(yplog), }
\NormalTok{           label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"$p\_t = k\_t\^{}\{"} \OperatorTok{+} \StringTok{"}\SpecialCharTok{\{:.2f\}}\StringTok{"}\NormalTok{.}\BuiltInTok{format}\NormalTok{(lm.coef\_[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{][}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{]) }\OperatorTok{+} \StringTok{"\}$"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.xlabel(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Rank $k\_t$\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.ylabel(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Probability $p\_t$\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.legend()}
\NormalTok{plt.show()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

So the fitted line is roughly:

\begin{eqnarray} 
    p_t & = & k_t^{-1.16} \\
    & = & \frac{1}{k_t^{1.16}}
\end{eqnarray}

Not exactly \(p_t = \frac{1}{k_t}\) as the Zipf's law suggests.

\subsection*{A.3. Probabilities in Training
Data}\label{a.3.-probabilities-in-training-data}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{A.3. Probabilities in Training Data}

Given the above dataset, let's split it into two random subsets: 80\%
training and 20\% testing.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# generate random true (80\% chance) or false values}
\NormalTok{train\_index }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.random.rand(}\BuiltInTok{len}\NormalTok{(emails)) }\OperatorTok{\textless{}} \FloatTok{0.8}
\CommentTok{\# use the above list to take the training (true values)}
\NormalTok{train }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ emails[train\_index]}
\CommentTok{\# the opposite (rest) is for testing}
\NormalTok{test }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ emails[}\OperatorTok{\textasciitilde{}}\NormalTok{train\_index]}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

The probability of a ham \(p(ham)\) in the training data:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# get the total number of instances}
\NormalTok{n }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ train.shape[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{]}
\CommentTok{\# get the total number of hams}
\NormalTok{counts }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ train.groupby(}\StringTok{"v1"}\NormalTok{)[}\StringTok{"v1"}\NormalTok{].value\_counts()}
\NormalTok{ham }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ counts[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{]}
\CommentTok{\# compute probability}
\NormalTok{p\_ham }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ ham }\OperatorTok{/}\NormalTok{ n}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"p(ham) = }\SpecialCharTok{\{:.3f\}}\StringTok{"}\NormalTok{.}\BuiltInTok{format}\NormalTok{(p\_ham))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

The probability of a spam \(p(spam)\):

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# get the total number of spams}
\NormalTok{spam }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ counts[}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{]}
\CommentTok{\# compute probability}
\NormalTok{p\_spam }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ spam }\OperatorTok{/}\NormalTok{ n}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"p(spam) = }\SpecialCharTok{\{:.3f\}}\StringTok{"}\NormalTok{.}\BuiltInTok{format}\NormalTok{(p\_spam))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Now we vectorize the training data:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{vect }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ CountVectorizer(analyzer}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"word"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{X\_train }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ vect.fit\_transform(train.v2)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

And use the trained dictionary to vectorize test data:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{X\_test }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ vect.transform(test.v2)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

We split the training data into spam and ham subsets:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{ham\_index }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ train[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}v1\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{]}\OperatorTok{==}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}ham\textquotesingle{}}
\NormalTok{hams }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ train[ham\_index]}
\NormalTok{spams }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ train[}\OperatorTok{\textasciitilde{}}\NormalTok{ham\_index]}
\NormalTok{X\_hams }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ vect.transform(hams.v2)}
\NormalTok{X\_spams }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ vect.transform(spams.v2)}
\NormalTok{X\_hams.shape}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Let's locate a term in the list of terms (dictionary):

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{terms }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ vect.get\_feature\_names()}
\NormalTok{t }\OperatorTok{=} \StringTok{"prize"}
\NormalTok{i }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ terms.index(t)}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"The }\SpecialCharTok{\{:d\}}\StringTok{th term is: }\SpecialCharTok{\{:s\}}\StringTok{"}\NormalTok{.}\BuiltInTok{format}\NormalTok{(i, t))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

We can compute conditional probabibility of a term in the ham
\(p(t|ham)\):

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{hamf }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ X\_hams.toarray().}\BuiltInTok{sum}\NormalTok{(axis}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{hamttf }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ hamf.}\BuiltInTok{sum}\NormalTok{()}
\NormalTok{f }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ hamf[i]}
\NormalTok{p }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ f }\OperatorTok{/}\NormalTok{ hamttf}
\NormalTok{ps }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ (f}\OperatorTok{+}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{)}\OperatorTok{/}\NormalTok{(hamttf}\OperatorTok{+}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{)}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"p(}\SpecialCharTok{\{:s\}}\StringTok{|ham) = }\SpecialCharTok{\{:.7f\}}\StringTok{, smoothed to }\SpecialCharTok{\{:.7f\}}\StringTok{"}\NormalTok{.}\BuiltInTok{format}\NormalTok{(t, p, ps))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

You see it is very unlikely for term \texttt{prize} to appear in a ham
(normal email).

Now let's look at the conditional probability of the same term in the
spam \(p(t|spam)\):

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{terms }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ vect.get\_feature\_names()}
\NormalTok{spamf }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ X\_spams.toarray().}\BuiltInTok{sum}\NormalTok{(axis}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{spamttf }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ spamf.}\BuiltInTok{sum}\NormalTok{()}
\NormalTok{f }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ spamf[i]}
\NormalTok{p }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ f }\OperatorTok{/}\NormalTok{ hamttf}
\NormalTok{ps }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ (f}\OperatorTok{+}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{)}\OperatorTok{/}\NormalTok{(spamttf}\OperatorTok{+}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{)}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"p(}\SpecialCharTok{\{:s\}}\StringTok{|spam) = }\SpecialCharTok{\{:.7f\}}\StringTok{, smoothed to }\SpecialCharTok{\{:.7f\}}\StringTok{"}\NormalTok{.}\BuiltInTok{format}\NormalTok{(t, p, ps))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

What do \(p(prize|ham)\) and \(p(prize|spam)\) mean respectively?

Apparently, it is much more likely to see \texttt{prize} in a spam than
in a ham, i.e.~\(p(prize|ham) \gg p(prize|spam)\).

Take a look at a few other terms (words) in spam vs.~ham to see if their
conditional probabilities make sense.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{t1 }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ terms.index(}\StringTok{"prize"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{t2 }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ terms.index(}\StringTok{"meeting"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{t1, t2}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# Example to plot two term (column vectors) on x and y}
\NormalTok{plt.plot(X\_hams.toarray()[:,t1], X\_hams.toarray()[:,t2], }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}+\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"blue"}\NormalTok{, label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"Hams"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.plot(X\_spams.toarray()[:,t1], X\_spams.toarray()[:,t2], }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}o\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"red"}\NormalTok{, label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"Spams"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.xlabel(}\StringTok{"prize"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.ylabel(}\StringTok{"meeting"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.legend()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\subsection*{A.4. Probabilistic Binary Model (Bernoulli Naive
Bayes)}\label{a.4.-probabilistic-binary-model-bernoulli-naive-bayes}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{A.4. Probabilistic Binary Model
(Bernoulli Naive Bayes)}

The above probabilities form the basis of the Naive Bayes model
(probabilistic classification).

Now let's train a Bernoulli Naive Bayes classifier, with binary term
occurrences:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ sklearn.naive\_bayes }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ BernoulliNB}

\CommentTok{\# build the Bernoulli Naive Bayes classifiers}
\CommentTok{\# with parameters, such as: }
\CommentTok{\# 1. alpha value for the Laplace estimator (smoothing)}
\CommentTok{\# 2. binarize, the threshold/cutoff for 0 or 1 values}
\NormalTok{bNB }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ BernoulliNB(alpha}\OperatorTok{=}\FloatTok{.01}\NormalTok{, binarize}\OperatorTok{=}\FloatTok{0.0}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{bNB.fit(X\_train, train.v1)}
\NormalTok{v1p }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ bNB.predict(X\_test)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(v1p)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Now let's compare and compute predicted values \(v1p\) and actual class
labels \(v1\) in the testing data. We first look at the confusion
matrix:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ sklearn.metrics }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ confusion\_matrix, plot\_confusion\_matrix}
\NormalTok{confusion\_matrix(v1p, test.v1)}
\NormalTok{display(plot\_confusion\_matrix(bNB, X\_test, test.v1, values\_format}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}d\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Look at the confusion matrix here and think about these questions: +
Among the four values, which one would you like to maximize for spam
filtering? + Which ones would you minimize?

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ sklearn.metrics }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ accuracy\_score, cohen\_kappa\_score }
\NormalTok{acc }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ accuracy\_score(v1p, test.v1)}
\NormalTok{kappa }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ cohen\_kappa\_score(v1p, test.v1)}

\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Accuracy: }\SpecialCharTok{\{:.4f\}}\StringTok{"}\NormalTok{.}\BuiltInTok{format}\NormalTok{(acc))}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Kappa   : }\SpecialCharTok{\{:.4f\}}\StringTok{"}\NormalTok{.}\BuiltInTok{format}\NormalTok{(kappa))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Adjust the parameters, train and test the data again. Does it make any
difference?

\subsection*{A.5. Probablistic Term Frequency Model (Multinomial Naive
Bayes)}\label{a.5.-probablistic-term-frequency-model-multinomial-naive-bayes}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{A.5. Probablistic Term Frequency Model
(Multinomial Naive Bayes)}

Likewise, we can train and test the Naive Bayes model with the
Multinomial distribution (term frequencies).

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ sklearn.naive\_bayes }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ MultinomialNB}

\CommentTok{\# build the Bernoulli Naive Bayes classifiers}
\CommentTok{\# with parameters, such as: }
\CommentTok{\# 1. alpha value for the Laplace estimator (smoothing)}
\NormalTok{mNB }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ MultinomialNB(alpha}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{mNB.fit(X\_train, train.v1)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Take a look at some of the probabilities:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ math}

\NormalTok{pt\_ham }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ mNB.feature\_log\_prob\_[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{][i]}
\NormalTok{pt\_spam }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ mNB.feature\_log\_prob\_[}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{][i]}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}p(}\SpecialCharTok{\{:s\}}\StringTok{|ham) = }\SpecialCharTok{\{:.7f\}}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{.}\BuiltInTok{format}\NormalTok{(t, math.exp(pt\_ham)))}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}p(}\SpecialCharTok{\{:s\}}\StringTok{|spam) = }\SpecialCharTok{\{:.7f\}}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{.}\BuiltInTok{format}\NormalTok{(t, math.exp(pt\_spam)))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Compare the estimated values to the same conditional probabilities we
obtained earlier. Are they similar, relatively?

Now let's use the trained model (estimated probabilities) to predit on
the test data:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{v1p }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ mNB.predict(X\_test)}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{()}
\NormalTok{display(plot\_confusion\_matrix(mNB, X\_test, test.v1, values\_format}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}d\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Evaluation with related metrics:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{acc }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ accuracy\_score(v1p, test.v1)}
\NormalTok{kappa }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ cohen\_kappa\_score(v1p, test.v1)}

\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Accuracy: }\SpecialCharTok{\{:.4f\}}\StringTok{"}\NormalTok{.}\BuiltInTok{format}\NormalTok{(acc))}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Kappa   : }\SpecialCharTok{\{:.4f\}}\StringTok{"}\NormalTok{.}\BuiltInTok{format}\NormalTok{(kappa))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Again, you may play with the alpha parameter (Laplace smoothing) for the
model. What model performs better?

Are accuracy and kappa good metrics for the evaluation here? Think about
what your objective is for email spam filtering and what your objectives
might be? Are there any other evaluation metrics -- precision, recall,
specificity, etc. -- that you should consider here?

\section*{B. Linear Classification
Model}\label{b.-linear-classification-model}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{B. Linear Classification Model}

\markright{B. Linear Classification Model}

Here is a small dataset about car evaluation:
\href{../data/cars.txt}{cars.txt}. It is originally from UCI's Car
Evaluation Data Set
(https://archive.ics.uci.edu/ml/datasets/Car+Evaluation) and has been
modified (\textbf{idealized} for now) for demonstration here.

We focus on each car's: 1. \textbf{Buying price} \(b\) 2.
\textbf{Maintenance cost} \(m\) 3. \textbf{Car evaluation}, i.e.~class
\(a\) with values of accceptable (\(1\)) or unacceptable \(0\).

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{cars }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ pd.read\_csv(}\StringTok{"../data/cars.txt"}\NormalTok{, sep}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{" "}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{display(cars[[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}b\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}m\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}a\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{]].sample(frac}\OperatorTok{=}\FloatTok{0.5}\NormalTok{))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Now take a look at the car's evaluation \(a\) (acceptable
vs.~unacceptable) on the two attribute dimensions, buying price \(b\)
and maintenance cost \(m\):

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{positive }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ cars[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}a\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{]}\OperatorTok{\textgreater{}}\DecValTok{0}
\NormalTok{cars0 }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ cars[}\OperatorTok{\textasciitilde{}}\NormalTok{positive]}
\NormalTok{cars1 }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ cars[positive]}
\CommentTok{\# for name, group in groups: }
\NormalTok{plt.plot(cars0.b, cars0.m, linestyle}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, marker}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}o\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }
\NormalTok{         color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"red"}\NormalTok{, ms}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{6}\NormalTok{, label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"Unacceptable"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.plot(cars1.b, cars1.m, linestyle}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, marker}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}+\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }
\NormalTok{         color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"blue"}\NormalTok{, ms}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{, label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"Acceptable"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.xlabel(}\StringTok{"Buying Price $b$"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.ylabel(}\StringTok{"Maintenance Cost $m$"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.legend(loc}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"center"}\NormalTok{, shadow}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.show()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\subsection*{B.1. Linear Classification (Perceptron) with Idealized
Data}\label{b.1.-linear-classification-perceptron-with-idealized-data}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{B.1. Linear Classification
(Perceptron) with Idealized Data}

Let's use the cars data to train a perceptron:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ sklearn.linear\_model }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ Perceptron}

\CommentTok{\# build a perceptron (single layer neural network)}
\CommentTok{\# with parameters: }
\CommentTok{\# 1. 20 iterations}
\CommentTok{\# 2. learning rate of 0.15}
\NormalTok{net1 }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ Perceptron(max\_iter}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{20}\NormalTok{, eta0}\OperatorTok{=}\FloatTok{0.15}\NormalTok{, random\_state}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{net1.fit(cars[[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}b\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}m\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{]], cars[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}a\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{])}
\NormalTok{w0 }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ net1.intercept\_[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{]}
\NormalTok{w1, w2 }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ net1.coef\_[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{]}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"The linear model is: }\SpecialCharTok{\{:.2f\}\{:+.2f\}}\StringTok{b}\SpecialCharTok{\{:+.2f\}}\StringTok{m = 0"}\NormalTok{.}\BuiltInTok{format}\NormalTok{(w0, w1, w2))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Now we can visualize the linear function, a line on the 2-dimensional
space:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# function to plot data with linear model}
\KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ plot\_linear(ax, title, cars0, cars1):}
    \KeywordTok{global}\NormalTok{ w0, w1, w2}
\NormalTok{    ax.plot(cars0.b, cars0.m, linestyle}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, marker}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}o\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }
\NormalTok{             color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"red"}\NormalTok{, alpha}\OperatorTok{=}\FloatTok{0.2}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    ax.plot(cars1.b, cars1.m, linestyle}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, marker}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}+\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }
\NormalTok{             color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"blue"}\NormalTok{, alpha}\OperatorTok{=}\FloatTok{0.2}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    x }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.array([}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\FloatTok{2.5}\NormalTok{])}
\NormalTok{    y }\OperatorTok{=} \OperatorTok{{-}}\NormalTok{(w0 }\OperatorTok{+}\NormalTok{ w1 }\OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{ x)}\OperatorTok{/}\NormalTok{w2}
\NormalTok{    ax.plot(x, y, color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"black"}\NormalTok{, linewidth}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{, label}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{title)}
\NormalTok{    ax.legend(loc}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"center"}\NormalTok{)}

\NormalTok{positive }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ cars[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}a\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{]}\OperatorTok{\textgreater{}}\DecValTok{0}
\NormalTok{cars0 }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ cars[}\OperatorTok{\textasciitilde{}}\NormalTok{positive]}
\NormalTok{cars1 }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ cars[positive]}
\NormalTok{plot\_linear(plt, }\StringTok{"20 Epochs"}\NormalTok{, cars0, cars1)}
\NormalTok{plt.show()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Now that the Perceptron takes 20 iterations to find the weight, let's go
through the iterations to see how the values of \([w_0, w_1, w_2]\) are
updated and converge.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ warnings}

\NormalTok{it, w0s, w1s, w2s }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ [], [], [], []}
\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ i }\KeywordTok{in} \BuiltInTok{range}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{16}\NormalTok{):}
    \ControlFlowTok{with}\NormalTok{ warnings.catch\_warnings():}
\NormalTok{        warnings.simplefilter(}\StringTok{"ignore"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{        net1 }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ Perceptron(max\_iter}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{i, eta0}\OperatorTok{=}\FloatTok{0.15}\NormalTok{, random\_state}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{) }\CommentTok{\#, warm\_start=True)}
\NormalTok{        net1.fit(cars[[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}b\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}m\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{]], cars[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}a\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{])}
\NormalTok{    it.append(i)}
\NormalTok{    w0s.append(net1.intercept\_[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{])}
\NormalTok{    w1s.append(net1.coef\_[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{][}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{]) }\CommentTok{\# / (net1.intercept\_[0] + 0.000000001))}
\NormalTok{    w2s.append(net1.coef\_[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{][}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{]) }\CommentTok{\# / (net1.intercept\_[0] + 0.000000001))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Now we can plot how the \(w\) values change over the iterations
(epochs):

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{fig, (ax1, ax2) }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ plt.subplots(}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{ax1.plot(it, w0s, label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"w\_0"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{ax1.legend(loc}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"lower right"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{ax2.plot(it, w1s, label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"w\_1"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{ax2.plot(it, w2s, label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"w\_2"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{ax2.}\BuiltInTok{set}\NormalTok{(xlabel}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"Epochs"}\NormalTok{)}
\CommentTok{\# ax1.ylabel("Values")}
\NormalTok{ax2.legend()}
\CommentTok{\# fig.show()}
\NormalTok{plt.show()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Here is the visualized evolution of the linear function (straight line)
over the iterations (epochs):

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{fig, axs2 }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ plt.subplots(}\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{) }
\NormalTok{i}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{0}
\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ axs }\KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ axs2:}
    \ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ ax }\KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ axs: }
\NormalTok{        ep }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ it[i]}
\NormalTok{        w0 }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ w0s[i]}
\NormalTok{        w1 }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ w1s[i]}
\NormalTok{        w2 }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ w2s[i]}
\NormalTok{        plot\_linear(ax, }\StringTok{"\#}\SpecialCharTok{\{0\}}\StringTok{"}\NormalTok{.}\BuiltInTok{format}\NormalTok{(ep), cars0, cars1)}
\NormalTok{        i }\OperatorTok{+=} \DecValTok{1}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Data}\label{b.2.-linear-classification-with-not-so-ideal-data}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{B.2. Linear Classification with
Not-So-Ideal Data}

Now if we simply add a \textbf{new data} point \((2,2,1)\) to the
idealized data:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{new }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ pd.DataFrame([[}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{]], columns}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}b\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}m\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}a\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{])}

\NormalTok{plt.plot(newcars1.b, newcars1.m, linestyle}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, marker}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}+\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }
\NormalTok{         color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"blue"}\NormalTok{, ms}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{, label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"Acceptable"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.xlabel(}\StringTok{"Buying Price"}\NormalTok{)}

\NormalTok{plt.legend(loc}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"center"}\NormalTok{, shadow}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.show()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Now our training data in \texttt{newcars} have a \textbf{new situation}
not encountered before.

Let's repeat the process: 1. Train and visualize the Perceptron model on
the \texttt{newcars} data (as we did on the \texttt{cars} data earlier).
2. Do you think we will obtain a good linear function (perceptron)?

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{net1 }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ Perceptron(max\_iter}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{100}\NormalTok{, eta0}\OperatorTok{=}\FloatTok{0.15}\NormalTok{, random\_state}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{net1.fit(newcars[[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}b\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}m\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{]], newcars[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}a\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{])}

\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Here is the model after 100 iterations (epochs):

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{plot\_linear(plt, }\StringTok{"100 Epochs"}\NormalTok{, newcars0, newcars1)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Even after many iterations (epochs) of training, the linear function
cannot seem to separate the two classes.

The \(w\) coefficients did converged but the results here are not as
sastifactory.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{it, w0s, w1s, w2s }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ [], [], [], []}
\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ i }\KeywordTok{in} \BuiltInTok{range}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{51}\NormalTok{):}
    \ControlFlowTok{with}\NormalTok{ warnings.catch\_warnings():}
\NormalTok{        warnings.simplefilter(}\StringTok{"ignore"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{        net1 }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ Perceptron(max\_iter}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{i, eta0}\OperatorTok{=}\FloatTok{0.15}\NormalTok{, random\_state}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{) }\CommentTok{\#, warm\_start=True)}
\NormalTok{        net1.fit(newcars[[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}b\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}m\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{]], newcars[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}a\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{])}
\NormalTok{    it.append(i)}
\NormalTok{    w0s.append(net1.intercept\_[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{])}
\NormalTok{    w1s.append(net1.coef\_[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{][}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{])}
\NormalTok{    w2s.append(net1.coef\_[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{][}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{])}

\NormalTok{fig, (ax1, ax2) }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ plt.subplots(}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{ax1.plot(it, w0s, label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"w\_0"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{ax1.legend(loc}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"lower right"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{ax2.plot(it, w1s, label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"w\_1"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{ax2.plot(it, w2s, label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"w\_2"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{ax2.}\BuiltInTok{set}\NormalTok{(xlabel}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"Epochs"}\NormalTok{)}

\section*{C. Non-Linear Model with Multi-layer Neural
Network}\label{c.-non-linear-model-with-multi-layer-neural-network}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{C. Non-Linear Model with Multi-layer
Neural Network}

\markright{C. Non-Linear Model with Multi-layer Neural Network}

Now that the data cannot be linear separated, let's try a Multi-layer
Neural Network (non-linear classifier) on the data:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ sklearn.neural\_network }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ MLPClassifier}
\NormalTok{net2 }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ MLPClassifier(hidden\_layer\_sizes}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{), activation}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}logistic\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }
\NormalTok{                     solver}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}lbfgs\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, max\_iter}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{100}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{net2.fit(newcars[[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}b\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}m\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{]], newcars[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}a\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{])}
\NormalTok{net2.predict(newcars[[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}b\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}m\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{]])}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

To better understand the model, let's use the notation here according to
the \href{../models/nonlinear_class.html}{Non-Linear Classification
Model} lecture:

Note: A difference in \texttt{MLPClassifier} we built here is the
\textbf{additional links} from \(v_1 \to h_2\) and \(v_2 \to h_1\) --
the above lecture/figure skips those links to simplify the calculation
and it still works.

\(v\) variables for the input: + \(v_1\): the buying price variable
\texttt{b} + \(v_2\): the maintenance cost variable \texttt{m}

\(h\) variables for the hidden layer (only one layer): + \(h_0 = f(1)\):
is the hidden layer for overall bias input. + \(h_1 = f(s_1)\): is
activation based on the weighted sum \(s_1\) to hidden node \(1\). +
\(h_2 = f(s_2)\): is activation based on the weighted sum \(s_2\) to
hidden node \(2\).

where \(f\) is the sigmoid (logistic) function for activation:

\[ f(s) = \frac{1}{1 + e^{-s}}\]

Based on the coefficients obtained from the above model, we have: + From
bias \(v_0\) to hidden layer:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(net2.intercepts\_[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{])}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  From \(v_1\) to hidden layer, i.e.~first layer (index \(0\)) and first
  variable (index \(0\)):
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(net2.coefs\_[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{][}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{])}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  From \(v_2\) to hidden layer, i.e.~first layer (index \(0\)) and
  second variable (index \(1\)):
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(net2.coefs\_[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{][}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{])}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Following the model, we define a general function for the sum:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ math}
\CommentTok{\# weight sum of input layer to a hidden node}
\CommentTok{\# v1 and v2 are input values}
\CommentTok{\# i is the index of the layer (0 from input to hidden, 1 from hidden to output)}
\CommentTok{\# j is the index of the node to receive the sum}
\KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ s(v1, v2, i, j): }
\NormalTok{    w0 }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ net2.intercepts\_[i][j]}
\NormalTok{    w1 }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ net2.coefs\_[i][}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{][j]}
\NormalTok{    w2 }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ net2.coefs\_[i][}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{][j]}
    \ControlFlowTok{return}\NormalTok{ w0 }\OperatorTok{+}\NormalTok{ w1}\OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{v1 }\OperatorTok{+}\NormalTok{ w2}\OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{v2}

\KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ sigmoid(s):}
    \ControlFlowTok{return} \DecValTok{1} \OperatorTok{/}\NormalTok{ (}\DecValTok{1} \OperatorTok{+}\NormalTok{ math.exp(}\OperatorTok{{-}}\NormalTok{s))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Hence, given input values such as \((v_1=2, v_2=2)\), the hidden nodes
receive the following weighted sums:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{s1 }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ s(}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{s2 }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ s(}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{)}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Weighted sum to hidden node 1: s1 = }\SpecialCharTok{\{:.2f\}}\StringTok{"}\NormalTok{.}\BuiltInTok{format}\NormalTok{(s1))}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Weighted sum to hidden node 2: s2 = }\SpecialCharTok{\{:.2f\}}\StringTok{"}\NormalTok{.}\BuiltInTok{format}\NormalTok{(s2))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Using the sigmoid function, the hidden layer activated values are:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{h1 }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ sigmoid(s1)}
\NormalTok{h2 }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ sigmoid(s2)}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Sigmoid activation of hidden node 1: h1 = }\SpecialCharTok{\{:.2f\}}\StringTok{"}\NormalTok{.}\BuiltInTok{format}\NormalTok{(h1))}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Sigmoid activation of hidden node 2: h2 = }\SpecialCharTok{\{:.2f\}}\StringTok{"}\NormalTok{.}\BuiltInTok{format}\NormalTok{(h2))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Now if we apply this process to all instances in the \texttt{newcars}
data:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# compute the hidden layer output}
\CommentTok{\# weighted sum}
\NormalTok{newcars[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}s1\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ s(newcars[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}b\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{], newcars[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}m\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{], }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{newcars[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}s2\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ s(newcars[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}b\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{], newcars[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}m\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{], }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{)}
\CommentTok{\# sigmoid transformation}
\NormalTok{newcars[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}h1\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{1}\OperatorTok{/}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{1} \OperatorTok{+}\NormalTok{ np.exp(}\OperatorTok{{-}}\NormalTok{ newcars[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}s1\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{])) }
\NormalTok{newcars[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}h2\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{1}\OperatorTok{/}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{1} \OperatorTok{+}\NormalTok{ np.exp(}\OperatorTok{{-}}\NormalTok{ newcars[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}s2\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{])) }

\CommentTok{\# compute the final output}
\CommentTok{\# weighted sum and then sigmoid transformation}
\NormalTok{newcars[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}so\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ s(newcars[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}h1\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{], newcars[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}h2\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{], }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{newcars[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}o\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{1}\OperatorTok{/}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{1} \OperatorTok{+}\NormalTok{ np.exp(}\OperatorTok{{-}}\NormalTok{ newcars[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}so\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{]))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

And we can plot the hidden layer result \(h_1\) and \(h_2\), which is a
non-linear transformation of \(v_1\) and \(v_2\) using a sigmoid
(logistic) function. You can see here that the data are now linearly
separable:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{positive }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ newcars[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}a\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{]}\OperatorTok{\textgreater{}}\DecValTok{0}
\NormalTok{newcars0 }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ newcars[}\OperatorTok{\textasciitilde{}}\NormalTok{positive]}
\NormalTok{newcars1 }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ newcars[positive]}
\CommentTok{\# for name, group in groups: }
\NormalTok{plt.plot(newcars0.h1, newcars0.h2, linestyle}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, marker}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}o\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }
\NormalTok{         color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"red"}\NormalTok{, alpha}\OperatorTok{=}\FloatTok{0.5}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.plot(newcars1.h1, newcars1.h2, linestyle}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, marker}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}+\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }
\NormalTok{         color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"blue"}\NormalTok{, alpha}\OperatorTok{=}\FloatTok{0.5}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.xlabel(}\StringTok{"Hidden Node $h\_1$"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.ylabel(}\StringTok{"Hidden Node $h\_2$"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{x }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.array([}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{])}

\NormalTok{w0 }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ net2.intercepts\_[}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{][}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{]}
\NormalTok{w1 }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ net2.coefs\_[}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{][}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{][}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{]}
\NormalTok{w2 }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ net2.coefs\_[}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{][}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{][}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{]}
\NormalTok{y }\OperatorTok{=} \OperatorTok{{-}}\NormalTok{w0}\OperatorTok{/}\NormalTok{w2 }\OperatorTok{{-}}\NormalTok{ w1 }\OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{ x }\OperatorTok{/}\NormalTok{ w2}

\NormalTok{equation }\OperatorTok{=} \StringTok{"$}\SpecialCharTok{\{:.0f\}\{:+.0f\}}\StringTok{h\_1}\SpecialCharTok{\{:+.0f\}}\StringTok{ h\_2 = 0$"}\NormalTok{.}\BuiltInTok{format}\NormalTok{(w0, w1, w2)}

\NormalTok{plt.plot(x, y, color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"black"}\NormalTok{, linewidth}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{, label}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{equation)}
\NormalTok{plt.legend()}
\NormalTok{plt.show()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

The solid line here is in fact the linear function obtained by the
Multi-layer Perceptron at the output layer where:

\[ \sum_i w_i h_i = 0\]

With the three \(w\) weights estimated here:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Weights (w0, w1, w2) at the output: (}\SpecialCharTok{\{:.0f\}}\StringTok{, }\SpecialCharTok{\{:.0f\}}\StringTok{, }\SpecialCharTok{\{:.0f\}}\StringTok{)"}\NormalTok{.}\BuiltInTok{format}\NormalTok{(w0, w1, w2))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

The final linear equation at the output can be written as:

\begin{eqnarray}
\sum_i w_i h_i & = & 9 - 18 h_1 - 17 h_2 \\
               & = & 0
\end{eqnarray}

which is the solid line in the above plot.

You see the multi-layer perceptron is essentially transforming data with
non-linear functions (e.g.~a sigmoid) -- sometimes multiple
transformations through multiple hidden layers -- and, in the end, use a
linear equation (weighted sum) to separate the transformed data.

\section*{Recovered activity}\label{sec-lab-data-preprocessing}

\markright{Recovered activity}

\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced jigsaw, arc=.35mm, bottomrule=.15mm, bottomtitle=1mm, breakable, colback=white, colbacktitle=quarto-callout-note-color!10!white, colframe=quarto-callout-note-color-frame, coltitle=black, left=2mm, leftrule=.75mm, opacityback=0, opacitybacktitle=0.6, rightrule=.15mm, title=\textcolor{quarto-callout-note-color}{\faInfo}\hspace{0.5em}{Historical source and execution note}, titlerule=0mm, toprule=.15mm, toptitle=1mm]
