\subsection*{Data Quality}\label{data-quality}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{Data Quality}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Today's real-world data likely origins from multiple, heterogeneous
  sources
\item
  Therefore, we are very likely to handle low-quality data
\item
  It is important to properly address data quality issues
\item
  Even simple descriptive analysis can be applied such that:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Sort all values in ascending or descending order

    \begin{enumerate}
    \def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
    \tightlist
    \item
      If all values are identical: no information. Ignore this variable!
    \item
      If some values occur abnormally with very high frequency (e.g.,
      ``Alabama'' in 20\% cases for state entry: need to examine whether
      it is caused by any interesting pattern (e.g., football fan
      survey) or error (e.g., default value)
    \end{enumerate}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  All techniques (e.g., data cleaning, integration, reduction,
  transformation, and discretization) related to data quality need to be
  properly addressed
\end{itemize}

In this exercise, we are going to utilize a dataset distributed as a
part of the Yelp Dataset Challenge (https://www.yelp.com/dataset). The
ultimate goal is to create a classifier to predict the usefulness of
each review by using various attributes. For instance, the count of
words can be a good indicator of the usefulness of reviews because
longer reviews can deliver more information than shorter reviews can do.
The sentiment of reviews might be important because too
positive/negative reviews might not be objective. Metrics related to
social networks such as degree, betweenness, and eigenvector might be
important by representing the social status of reviewers. To achieve the
ultimate goal, preprocessing data is the first step. Let's open the
data. There are 1,000 tuples and 26 attributes. The names of attributes
are self-explanatory.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\#\# Imports all necessary modules}
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ pandas }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ pd}
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ matplotlib.pyplot }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ plt}
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ numpy }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ np}
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ seaborn }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ sns}
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ numpy }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ linalg}
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ scipy }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ stats}

\CommentTok{\#\# This loads the csv file from disk}
\NormalTok{yelp\_data }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ pd.read\_csv(}
\NormalTok{    filepath\_or\_buffer }\OperatorTok{=} \StringTok{"../data/Yelp\_Usefulness\_Practice.csv"}\NormalTok{, sep }\OperatorTok{=} \StringTok{","}\NormalTok{, header}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{ )}

\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(yelp\_data.head(}\DecValTok{20}\NormalTok{))}
\CommentTok{\#\# print the dimension of the data}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(yelp\_data.shape)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\subsection*{Applying descriptive
analysis}\label{applying-descriptive-analysis}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{Applying descriptive analysis}

As explained earlier, we can apply a descriptive analysis to understand
the distribution of data. We can count the number of unique values as
well.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\#\# Set the maximum number of columns as "None" to display all columns}
\NormalTok{pd.set\_option(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}display.max\_columns\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\VariableTok{None}\NormalTok{)}

\CommentTok{\#\# Pandas\textquotesingle{} describe method can provide basic statistics for numeric variables at default}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(yelp\_data.describe())}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\#\# In the above, only 24 attributes got results.}
\CommentTok{\#\# We need to select "only" categorical attributes to get descriptive statistics for categorical attributes}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(yelp\_data[[}\StringTok{"review\_id"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"class"}\NormalTok{]].describe())}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

For the categorical attributes, we found that there are no cases such
that all values are identical or some values occur abnormally with very
high frequency.

However, in the case of numeric attributes, we found that there are
three missing values in the ``liking'' attribute. The total count of
tuples is 997, although all other attributes' count is 1,000. We can
also check missing values by using Pandas' isna method. Also,
``dislike'' attribute has all zero values. That said, we need to drop
this attribute by using Pandas' drop method. However, we will keep this
for now.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\#\# Show the number of data per attributes that has missing values}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(yelp\_data.isna().}\BuiltInTok{sum}\NormalTok{())}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\#\# check the missing values across tuples of the "liking" attribute}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(yelp\_data[}\StringTok{"liking"}\NormalTok{].isna())}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Now, let's create a box plot to see the distribution of the ``liking''
attribute. This will help us to determine how to handle the missing
data.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\#\# Initialize a 6 x 6in figure}
\NormalTok{fig }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ plt.figure(figsize }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ (}\DecValTok{6}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{6}\NormalTok{))}

\CommentTok{\#\# Make the box plot}
\NormalTok{plt.boxplot(}
    \CommentTok{\#\# We beed to drop tuples that include missing values}
\NormalTok{    yelp\_data[}\StringTok{"liking"}\NormalTok{][}\OperatorTok{\textasciitilde{}}\NormalTok{yelp\_data[}\StringTok{"liking"}\NormalTok{].isna()], labels }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ [}\StringTok{"Liking"}\NormalTok{]}
\NormalTok{)}

\CommentTok{\#\# Adjust the tick and label font size}
\NormalTok{plt.tick\_params(labelsize }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{15}\NormalTok{)}

\CommentTok{\#\# Set the title}
\NormalTok{plt.title(}\StringTok{"The normalized number of words related to \textquotesingle{}liking\textquotesingle{} sentiment"}\NormalTok{, fontsize }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{15}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

It seems that most of the values are zeros except for one tuple. It is
skewed. Therefore, it might be better to use the median central
tendency.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\#\# We should exclude missing tuples to get median}
\NormalTok{median }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.median(yelp\_data[}\StringTok{"liking"}\NormalTok{][}\OperatorTok{\textasciitilde{}}\NormalTok{yelp\_data[}\StringTok{"liking"}\NormalTok{].isna()])}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{ (}\StringTok{"The median is: "}\NormalTok{, median)}

\CommentTok{\#\# Replace missing values with median }
\NormalTok{yelp\_data[}\StringTok{"liking"}\NormalTok{].fillna(median, inplace}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{)}

\CommentTok{\#\# Check whether the missing value were filled}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(yelp\_data.isna().}\BuiltInTok{sum}\NormalTok{())}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\subsection*{Outlier Detection}\label{outlier-detection}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{Outlier Detection}

We can use Z-score and IQR score to detect ourliers. There are other
methods, too.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\#\# The first attribute (review\_id) and the last attribute (class) are categorical}
\CommentTok{\#\# Therefore, we cannot calculate z{-}score }
\CommentTok{\#\# The "dislike" attribute has 0 mean and sd. Therefore, we cannot calculate z score.}
\CommentTok{\#\# We create indices that exclude review\_id, dislike, and class}
\NormalTok{positions }\OperatorTok{=} \BuiltInTok{list}\NormalTok{(}\BuiltInTok{range}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{13}\NormalTok{))}
\NormalTok{positions.extend(}\BuiltInTok{list}\NormalTok{(}\BuiltInTok{range}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{14}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{25}\NormalTok{)))}

\NormalTok{z }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.}\BuiltInTok{abs}\NormalTok{(stats.zscore(yelp\_data.iloc[:, positions]))}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(z)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

The above numbers represent z scores of our data. It is difficult to say
which data point is an outlier. Let's try and define a threshold to
identify an outlier. To be conservative, we will use 3 as a threshold.
We can exclude 0.3\% of data.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{threshold }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{3}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(np.where(z }\OperatorTok{\textgreater{}} \DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

The first array contains the list of row numbers, and the second array
corresponding column numbers that have a Z-score higher than 3, which
mean they are: z{[}7{]}{[}1{]}, z{[}18{]}{[}17{]}, z{[}18{]}{[}23{]},
and so on.

We may want to remove tuples in which the most attributes are outliers.
We can remove all tuples regardless of the number of attributes that are
outliers. For this exercise, we adopt a very conservative approach.
Therefore, we count the frequency of tuples that includes outliers.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{unique\_elements, counts\_elements }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.unique(np.where(z }\OperatorTok{\textgreater{}} \DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{)[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{], return\_counts}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{)}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(np.asarray((unique\_elements, counts\_elements)))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

The first list represents the row number when the z score is larger than
3. The second list represents the frequency of the corresponding row.
The row 69 and 262 have the largest number of outliers. Therefore, we
want to remove these tuples from the dataset.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{yelp\_data }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ yelp\_data.drop([ }\DecValTok{69}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{262}\NormalTok{])}

\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(yelp\_data.shape)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

As you can see, two tuples were dropped. I will leave outlier detection
exercise using IQR to you.

\subsection*{Data Integration}\label{data-integration}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{Data Integration}

One potential problem in data integration is redundancies in attributes
and/or tuples. As for attributes, we can check redundancies by applying
correlation analysis.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{pcorr }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ yelp\_data.corr(method}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}pearson\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}

\CommentTok{\#\# Set the maximum number of columns as "None" to display all columns}
\NormalTok{pd.set\_option(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}display.max\_columns\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\VariableTok{None}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{pcorr}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

It is hard to see. We can use a heatmap to visualize the correlation
coefficients.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{plt.figure(figsize}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{12}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{))}
\NormalTok{sns.heatmap(pcorr, annot}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{, cmap}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{plt.cm.Reds)}
\NormalTok{plt.show()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

We found the strongest correlation (0.94) between degree and
eigenvector. The degree and betweenness are also strongly correlated
(0.83). These results represent redundancies in attributes. We can drop
one or two of them. However, we will leave it to the feature subset
selection method in this exercise.

\subsection*{Data Integration 2}\label{data-integration-2}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{Data Integration 2}

One common challenge in data integration is attribute construction. New
attributes need to be constructed from the given ones if existing
attributes do not adequately reflect the target label/value. For
instance, the ``area'' is a better indicator of housing prices than the
``length'' and ``width'' of a house. We observed that attributes related
to sentiment are sparse. Most of them are zeros. We will create new
attributes from the given ones. The attributes in the left of the arrow
mark are existing ones, and the attributes in the right are new ones. +
joy, love, affection, liking, enthusiasm -\textgreater{}
positive\_emotion + sadness, dislike, despair, horror, distress
-\textgreater{} negative\_emotion

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{yelp\_data[}\StringTok{"positive\_emotion"}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ yelp\_data[}\StringTok{"joy"}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{+}\NormalTok{ yelp\_data[}\StringTok{"love"}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{+}\NormalTok{ yelp\_data[}\StringTok{"affection"}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{+}\NormalTok{ yelp\_data[}\StringTok{"liking"}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{+}\NormalTok{ yelp\_data[}\StringTok{"enthusiasm"}\NormalTok{]}
\NormalTok{yelp\_data[}\StringTok{"negative\_emotion"}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ yelp\_data[}\StringTok{"sadness"}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{+}\NormalTok{ yelp\_data[}\StringTok{"dislike"}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{+}\NormalTok{ yelp\_data[}\StringTok{"despair"}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{+}\NormalTok{ yelp\_data[}\StringTok{"horror"}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{+}\NormalTok{ yelp\_data[}\StringTok{"distress"}\NormalTok{]}

\CommentTok{\#\# Drop existing attributes}
\NormalTok{yelp\_data }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ yelp\_data.drop([}\StringTok{"joy"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"love"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"affection"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"liking"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"enthusiasm"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"sadness"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"dislike"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"despair"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"horror"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"distress"}\NormalTok{], }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{)    }

\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(yelp\_data.head())}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\subsection*{Dimensionality Reduction +
Normalization}\label{dimensionality-reduction-normalization}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{Dimensionality Reduction +
Normalization}

As a method of data reduction, we can apply dimensionality reduction. We
covered PCA and feature subset selection in the lecture. In this
exercise, we will cover feature subset selection. We are also going to
apply normalization before selecting a feature subset. We will implement
the forward selection and bi-directional stepwise selection.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\#\# Create feature matrix by dropping the review\_id and label attribute}
\CommentTok{\#\# Review\_id is not going to helpful to predict the usefulness of reviews}
\NormalTok{X }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ yelp\_data.drop([}\StringTok{"review\_id"}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{"class"}\NormalTok{], }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{)    }

\CommentTok{\#\# Pre{-}processing. Sklearn takes integer as label}
\CommentTok{\# \#\# Create target attribute}
\NormalTok{yelp\_data[yelp\_data[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}class\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{==} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}useful\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{1}
\NormalTok{yelp\_data[yelp\_data[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}class\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{==} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}not\_useful\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{0}

\CommentTok{\# \#\# Specify the data type. Before specifying, the type was unknown}
\NormalTok{y }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ yelp\_data[}\StringTok{"class"}\NormalTok{].astype(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}int\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}

\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(yelp\_data[}\StringTok{"class"}\NormalTok{])}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(X.shape[}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{])}

\CommentTok{\#\# import the necessary libraries}
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ mlxtend.feature\_selection }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ SequentialFeatureSelector }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ SFS}
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ sklearn.naive\_bayes }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ MultinomialNB}
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ sklearn }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ preprocessing}

\CommentTok{\#\# Apply Min\_Max Scaler}
\NormalTok{min\_max\_scaler }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ preprocessing.MinMaxScaler()}
\NormalTok{X\_minmax }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ min\_max\_scaler.fit\_transform(X)}
\NormalTok{X\_minmax }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ pd.DataFrame(X\_minmax)}

\CommentTok{\#\# Sequential Forward Selection(sfs)}
\CommentTok{\#\# Please refer to http://rasbt.github.io/mlxtend/user\_guide/feature\_selection/SequentialFeatureSelector/\#sequential{-}feature{-}selector}
\CommentTok{\#\# for the detailed arguments options}
\NormalTok{sfs }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ SFS(MultinomialNB(),}
\NormalTok{           k\_features}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{,X\_minmax.shape[}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{]),}
\NormalTok{           forward}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{, }
\NormalTok{           floating}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{False}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{           scoring }\OperatorTok{=} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}r2\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{           cv }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{)}

\NormalTok{sfs.fit(X, y)}
\CommentTok{\#\# Get the final set of features}
\NormalTok{sfs.k\_feature\_names\_     }
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ mlxtend.plotting }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ plot\_sequential\_feature\_selection }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ plot\_sfs}
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ matplotlib.pyplot }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ plt}

\CommentTok{\#\# performance graph (y{-}axis: r{-}squared values)}
\NormalTok{fig }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ plot\_sfs(sfs.get\_metric\_dict(), kind}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}std\_dev\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.title(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Sequential Forward Selection (w. StdErr)\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.grid()}
\NormalTok{plt.show()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Thirteen attributes were selected. Let's try bi-directional stepwise
selection (combination of forward and backward selection) and compare
results.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\#\# Bi{-}directional stepwise selection}
\CommentTok{\#\# Please note that floating option was changed to "True"}
\NormalTok{sfs1 }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ SFS(MultinomialNB(),}
\NormalTok{           k\_features}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{,X\_minmax.shape[}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{]),}
\NormalTok{           forward}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{, }
\NormalTok{           floating}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{           scoring }\OperatorTok{=} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}r2\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{           cv }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{)}

\NormalTok{sfs1.fit(X, y)}
\CommentTok{\# Get the final set of features}
\NormalTok{sfs1.k\_feature\_names\_     }
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\#\# performance graph (y{-}axis: r{-}squared values)}
\NormalTok{fig1 }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ plot\_sfs(sfs1.get\_metric\_dict(), kind}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}std\_dev\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.title(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Sequential Bi{-}directional Stepwise Selection (w. StdErr)\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.grid()}
\NormalTok{plt.show()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Now, we have seven attributes
(`review\_stars',`word\_count',`correct\_spell\_ratio',`price\_included',`eigenvector',`positive\_emotion',`negative\_emotion').
We have a reduced set of attributes. The performance from both selection
methods seems to be not very different. Potentially, using a small set
will be better if the performance is not very different? However, it is
still early to conclude. We did not separate data into training and
testing sets. Also, what is the goal of your classifier? Is it
effectiveness or efficiency? Usually, we need to sacrifice one to
achieve another. In other words, effectiveness increases when efficiency
decreases, and vice versa. We will cover these topics later.

\section*{A. A NORMAL Spender Model}\label{a.-a-normal-spender-model}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{A. A NORMAL Spender Model}

\markright{A. A NORMAL Spender Model}

\subsection*{Mean and deviation}\label{mean-and-deviation}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{Mean and deviation}

Suppose, on average you purchase \(\mu=20\) dollars with deviation
\(\sigma=10\):

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{mu, sigma }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{20}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{10}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Here we set the mean \(\mu\) and standard deviation \(\sigma\) to 20 and
10 respectively.

\subsection*{Normal Distribution
(Model)}\label{normal-distribution-model}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{Normal Distribution (Model)}

Let's simulate 100 purchases you \emph{may} make following a
\textbf{normal} (Gaussian) distribution:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{purchases }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.random.normal(mu, sigma, }\DecValTok{100}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

We use the NumPy's normal distribution model to generate the 100
purchase amounts, just like what you could have done.

Now we show the distribution of your data using a histogram:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{count, bins, ignored }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ plt.hist(purchases, }\DecValTok{20}\NormalTok{, density}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{, color}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{(}\FloatTok{0.1}\NormalTok{, }\FloatTok{0.1}\NormalTok{, }\FloatTok{0.1}\NormalTok{, }\FloatTok{0.1}\NormalTok{), edgecolor}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"black"}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\subsection*{Normal Density
Distribution}\label{normal-density-distribution}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{Normal Density Distribution}

Compare to the normal distribution density function:

\begin{eqnarray}
f(x) & = & \frac{1}{\sigma \sqrt{2\pi}} e^{- \left( {x - \mu } \right)^2 / 2\sigma^2}
\end{eqnarray}

Now that we assume the data follow a normal distribution -- and it does
-- the data should follow the normal distribution curve. So let's
compare the histogram to the NORMAL model that generates the data.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{count, bins, ignored }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ plt.hist(purchases, }\DecValTok{20}\NormalTok{, density}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{, color}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{(}\FloatTok{0.1}\NormalTok{, }\FloatTok{0.1}\NormalTok{, }\FloatTok{0.1}\NormalTok{, }\FloatTok{0.1}\NormalTok{), edgecolor}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"black"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.plot(bins, }\DecValTok{1}\OperatorTok{/}\NormalTok{(sigma }\OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{ np.sqrt(}\DecValTok{2} \OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{ np.pi)) }\OperatorTok{*}
\NormalTok{                np.exp( }\OperatorTok{{-}}\NormalTok{ (bins }\OperatorTok{{-}}\NormalTok{ mu)}\OperatorTok{**}\DecValTok{2} \OperatorTok{/}\NormalTok{ (}\DecValTok{2} \OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{ sigma}\OperatorTok{**}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{)),}
\NormalTok{                linewidth}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{, color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}b\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.show()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

The blue curve depicts the MODEL, i.e.~the normal distribution where the
data come from. But it is easy to tell that the actual data, shown as
the histogram, do not form the exact bell shape of the model.

The actual spending, for example, may tend to have more purchases with a
greater amount, than what the model suggests. This is because the model
is probabilistic and comes with a degree of randomness. That leads to
the variance in the data and unpredictability of individual data
instances.

And this is the \textbf{noise}, which may appear to be outliers but, in
fact, comes from the one same model that generates all data here.

In terms of the distribution here, it is very unlikely that you will
spend more than 50 dollar on an order:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ scipy.stats }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ norm}
\NormalTok{normal\_spender }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ norm(mu, sigma)}
\NormalTok{prob }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{1}\OperatorTok{{-}}\NormalTok{normal\_spender.cdf(}\DecValTok{50}\NormalTok{)}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"How likely is an order of over 50 dollars? }\SpecialCharTok{\{:.2f\}}\StringTok{"}\NormalTok{.}\BuiltInTok{format}\NormalTok{(prob))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\section*{B. Outliers in Belgium International Phone
Calls}\label{b.-outliers-in-belgium-international-phone-calls}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{B. Outliers in Belgium International
Phone Calls}

\markright{B. Outliers in Belgium International Phone Calls}

The number of international phone calls (millions) from Belgium during
1950 - 1973:

\subsection*{Data}\label{data-1}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{Data}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{phones }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ pd.read\_csv(}\StringTok{"../data/phones.csv"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{phones[[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}year\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}calls\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{]].head(}\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\subsection*{Statistics}\label{statistics}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{Statistics}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{phones[[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}year\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}calls\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{]].describe()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\subsection*{Distribution}\label{distribution}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{Distribution}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{sns.distplot(phones[}\StringTok{"calls"}\NormalTok{], color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"gray"}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\subsection*{Potential Correlation}\label{potential-correlation}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{Potential Correlation}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{phones[[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}year\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}calls\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{]].corr(method}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"pearson"}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\subsection*{Linear Regression}\label{linear-regression-1}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{Linear Regression}

LSM, least Squares from the mean: + Least \textbf{mean} squares to be
minimized + Tries to fit all values

If we conduct a linear regression based on the classic method of least
squares, which is to minimize the sum of squared errors from the
\textbf{mean}, we obtain:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ sklearn.linear\_model }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ LinearRegression}

\NormalTok{x }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ phones[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}year\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{].values.reshape(}\OperatorTok{{-}}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{y }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ phones[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}calls\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{].values.reshape(}\OperatorTok{{-}}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{)}

\KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ lsm\_mean():}
\NormalTok{    lsm }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ LinearRegression()}
\NormalTok{    lsm.fit(x, y)}
    \CommentTok{\# print(lsm.intercept\_, lsm.coef\_)}
    \KeywordTok{global}\NormalTok{ yp }
\NormalTok{    yp }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ lsm.predict(x)}

    \CommentTok{\# plt.scatter(x, y,  color=\textquotesingle{}gray\textquotesingle{})}
\NormalTok{    plt.plot(x, yp, color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}red\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, linewidth}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    plt.xlabel(}\StringTok{"Year"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    plt.ylabel(}\StringTok{"\# Calls (tens of millions)"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    plt.show()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{lsm\_mean()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

The linear regression model shows the positive relation between year and
the number of phone calls. Over time, there is an increasing number of
calls from Belgium, which makes sense.

Again, the regression model here is to minimize the sum of least squared
errors, from the mean. That is, the model try to fit the regression line
along the mean or average over the years.

\subsubsection*{Robust Linear
Regression}\label{robust-linear-regression}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsubsection}{Robust Linear Regression}

LAD, least absolute deviation from the median: + A quantile regression,
with q=0.5 (median) + Robust regression models + \textbf{Median},
instead of mean, deviation + \textbf{Least affected by extreme values},
e.g.~outliers

An alternative regression technique is refered to as the Quantile
Regression, which is part of a family of techniques for robust
regression. We know that the quantitle at 0.5 or 50\% is the middle of
all values and is the median. So a quantile regression with q=0.5 is
``median regression,'' based on least absolute deviation.

In this case, when fitting the model, values and errors in the middle
have a greater impact on the regression model. Extreme values, which are
at the two ends of a distribution, will hardly have an impact on the
median error estimate. So outliers will be less likely to impact the
results.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ statsmodels.api }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ sm}
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ statsmodels.formula.api }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ smf}

\KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ lad\_median():}
    \CommentTok{\# rlm = sm.RLM(x, y, M=sm.robust.norms.HuberT())}
\NormalTok{    rlm }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ smf.quantreg(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}calls \textasciitilde{} year\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, phones)}
\NormalTok{    rlm\_res }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ rlm.fit(q}\OperatorTok{=}\FloatTok{0.5}\NormalTok{)}
    \CommentTok{\# print(rlm\_res.summary())}
    \KeywordTok{global}\NormalTok{ yp2}
\NormalTok{    yp2 }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ rlm\_res.params[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{+}\NormalTok{ x }\OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{ rlm\_res.params[}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{]}

    \CommentTok{\# plt.scatter(x, y,  color=\textquotesingle{}gray\textquotesingle{})}
\NormalTok{    plt.xlabel(}\StringTok{"Year"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    plt.ylabel(}\StringTok{"\# Calls (millions)"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    plt.plot(x, yp, color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}red\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, linewidth}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{, label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"Least Square (Mean)"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    plt.plot(x, yp2, color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}blue\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, linewidth}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{, label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"Least Deviation (Median)"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    plt.legend()}
\NormalTok{    plt.show()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{lad\_median()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Now after we perform the ``median regression'', we can compare its
regression line (the blue one) to the regression based on least mean
squares (the red line). The two lines are very different. In particular
, the least mean square model has a much steep regression line. What
happened?

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ show\_data():}
\NormalTok{    plt.scatter(x, y,  color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}gray\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"Data"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    plt.xlabel(}\StringTok{"Year"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    plt.ylabel(}\StringTok{"\# Calls (millions)"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    plt.plot(x, yp, color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}red\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, linewidth}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{, label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"Least Square (Mean)"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    plt.plot(x, yp2, color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}blue\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, linewidth}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{, label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"Least Deviation (Median)"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    plt.legend()}
\NormalTok{    plt.show()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{show\_data()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Now if we plot the actual data, the figure gives us some clues about
what make the difference.

As shown in the data, there are anomalies in the data, specifically
during years 1964 - 1969, where the numbers of calls appear to be
unsually greater.

The model based on least mean squares, the red line, is fitting the
regression line in terms of mean squared errors. The mean, as we know,
is affected by all values. So essentially, the least mean square model
is trying to ``please'' every single data points and the group of
outliers will have a major impact on the final regression line, which
goes in between the normal data points and outliers.

The ``median regression'' (so to speak), the blue line, is immune to the
outliers because it relies on the median deviation. As you can see, it
fits the normal data points very well.

But what are the outliers? What do they represent? Where do they come
from?

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ show\_data\_groups():}
\NormalTok{    phones1 }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ phones[phones[}\StringTok{"calls"}\NormalTok{]}\OperatorTok{\textless{}}\DecValTok{100}\NormalTok{]}
\NormalTok{    plt.scatter(phones1[}\StringTok{"year"}\NormalTok{], phones1[}\StringTok{"calls"}\NormalTok{],  color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}black\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"Number of Calls"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    phones2 }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ phones[phones[}\StringTok{"calls"}\NormalTok{]}\OperatorTok{\textgreater{}=}\DecValTok{100}\NormalTok{]}
\NormalTok{    plt.scatter(phones2[}\StringTok{"year"}\NormalTok{], phones2[}\StringTok{"calls"}\NormalTok{],  color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}red\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"Calls in Minutes (mistaken)"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    plt.xlabel(}\StringTok{"Year"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    plt.ylabel(}\StringTok{"\# Calls (millions)"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    plt.legend()}
\NormalTok{    plt.show()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{show\_data\_groups()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

In our definition of outliers, we declare that they must have come from
a process or model different from the one that generates the normal
data. So if the normal data points come from the number of phone calls
Belgium people make? What the outliers?

It turns out that the outliers are still about phone calls from people
in Belgium. However, they were mistakenly recorded as the number of
minutes, instead of counts of individual calls. So while the black
normal data points come from a ``counting'' process, and the red
outliers come from the time log. They are indeed two separate models.

\section*{C. Parametric Detection: What is a NORMAL
temperature?}\label{c.-parametric-detection-what-is-a-normal-temperature}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{C. Parametric Detection: What is a NORMAL
temperature?}

\markright{C. Parametric Detection: What is a NORMAL temperature?}

As we discussed, outliers come from a different process or model that
generates normal data. These are generative models or stochastic
(random) processes hidden behind the data. The idea here is that if we
can find out the model that produce normal data, we will then be able to
estimate the likelihood or probability of potential outliers.

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Parametric} methods: data are generated by a distribution
  \texttt{model} with parameters.
\item
  \textbf{Nonparametric} methods: do not assume a model but try to
  determine it \texttt{from\ data}.
\end{enumerate}

Example, temperature data in °C:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{ x }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ [}\FloatTok{24.0}\NormalTok{, }\FloatTok{28.9}\NormalTok{, }\FloatTok{28.9}\NormalTok{, }\FloatTok{29.0}\NormalTok{, }\FloatTok{29.1}\NormalTok{, }\FloatTok{29.1}\NormalTok{, }\FloatTok{29.2}\NormalTok{, }\FloatTok{29.2}\NormalTok{, }\FloatTok{29.3}\NormalTok{, }\FloatTok{29.4}\NormalTok{]}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
<img src="../figures/outliers/summer.jpg" style="width:100%;" alt="Summer">
<div style="position:absolute; top:50%; left:50%; transform:translate(-50%, -50%); font-size:48pt;">
    <font style="color:blue; background-color: white;">0&deg;C</font>
</div>
\end{verbatim}

Given the above sample data and the NORMAL assumption, how likely are
the following? 1. A temperature \textbf{below 0 °C}? 2. A temperature
\textbf{above 30 °C}?

Are the values normal or outliers?

\subsection*{1. Estimate Mean and Deviation from
Sample}\label{estimate-mean-and-deviation-from-sample}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{1. Estimate Mean and Deviation from
Sample}

Given normal distribution, two parameters to be estimated: 1. Mean:
\(\hat{\mu} = \bar{x} = \frac{1}{n} \sum_{i=1}^{n} x_i\) 2. Variance:
\(\hat{\sigma}^2 = \frac{1}{n} \sum_{i=1}^{n} (x_i - \bar{x})^2\)

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ math}
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ numpy }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ np}
\NormalTok{mu }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.mean(x)}
\NormalTok{std }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.std(x)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Mean = }\SpecialCharTok{\{:.2f\}}\StringTok{"}\NormalTok{.}\BuiltInTok{format}\NormalTok{(mu))}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Deviation = }\SpecialCharTok{\{:.2f\}}\StringTok{"}\NormalTok{.}\BuiltInTok{format}\NormalTok{(std))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\subsection*{2. Build the Normal Model with Estimated
Parameters}\label{build-the-normal-model-with-estimated-parameters}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{2. Build the Normal Model with
Estimated Parameters}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ scipy.stats }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ norm}
\NormalTok{model }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ norm(mu, std)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\subsection*{3. Compute the Likelihood of Temperature
Ranges}\label{compute-the-likelihood-of-temperature-ranges}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{3. Compute the Likelihood of
Temperature Ranges}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{t1,t2 }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{30}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"How likely is a temperature below 0 C?"}\NormalTok{, model.cdf(t1))}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"How likely is a temperature above 30 C?"}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{1}\OperatorTok{{-}}\NormalTok{model.cdf(t2))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Given the observed temperature data, \(t_1=0\) is extremely unlikley to
be generated by the normal distribution model here and is most likely an
outlier.

\section*{D. NORMAL Age in Context}\label{d.-normal-age-in-context}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{D. NORMAL Age in Context}

\markright{D. NORMAL Age in Context}

Some data do not appear to be outlier, until they are viewed in the
context. Let's look at the normal \texttt{age} of a working person in
the context of demographical groups.

\subsection*{1. Census Income Data}\label{census-income-data}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{1. Census Income Data}

The data are based on the 1994 Census of the U.S.

https://archive.ics.uci.edu/ml/datasets/Adult

The dataset comes with attributes including age, education, sex, and
income levels.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{people }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ pd.read\_csv(}\StringTok{"../data/adult.data"}\NormalTok{, header}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{None}\NormalTok{, skipinitialspace}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{people.columns }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ [}\StringTok{"age"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"workclass"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"fnlwgt"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"education"}\NormalTok{, }
                  \StringTok{"education\_num"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"marital\_status"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"occupation"}\NormalTok{, }
                  \StringTok{"relationship"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"race"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"sex"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"capital\_gain"}\NormalTok{, }
                  \StringTok{"capital\_loss"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"hours\_per\_week"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"native\_country"}\NormalTok{,}
                  \StringTok{"income"}
\NormalTok{                 ]}
\NormalTok{people.head()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{people.shape}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{sns.distplot(people[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}age\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{], bins}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{20}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

What is the chance of a working 18-year old or younger? Assume a normal
distribution.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{mu, std }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ norm.fit(people[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}age\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{])}
\NormalTok{normal\_age }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ norm(mu, std)}
\NormalTok{p }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ normal\_age.cdf(}\DecValTok{18}\NormalTok{)}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"}\SpecialCharTok{\{:.2f\}}\StringTok{"}\NormalTok{.}\BuiltInTok{format}\NormalTok{(p))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

The probability is about \(7\%\). It is less common but not necessarily
abnormal.

\subsection*{2. Working Age of Male
vs.~Female}\label{working-age-of-male-vs.-female}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{2. Working Age of Male vs.~Female}

Age distributions for \textbf{male vs.~female}:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{counts }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ people.groupby(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}sex\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{).size()}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(counts)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{male }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ people[(people[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}sex\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{==} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Male\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)]}
\NormalTok{female }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ people[(people[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}sex\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{==} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Female\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)]}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(male.shape, female.shape)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# male\_age = male[\textquotesingle{}age\textquotesingle{}]}
\CommentTok{\# female\_age = female[\textquotesingle{}age\textquotesingle{}]}
\CommentTok{\# male\_age.hist(histtype=\textquotesingle{}stepfilled\textquotesingle{}, color=\textquotesingle{}blue\textquotesingle{}, alpha=0.5, bins=20)}
\CommentTok{\# female\_age.hist(histtype=\textquotesingle{}stepfilled\textquotesingle{}, color="red", alpha=0.5, bins=20)}
\NormalTok{sns.distplot(male[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}age\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{], color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}blue\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, bins}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{20}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{sns.distplot(female[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}age\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{], color}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}red\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, bins}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{20}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# female\_age.hist(density=True, histtype=\textquotesingle{}stepfilled\textquotesingle{}, color="red", alpha=0.5, bins=20)}
\CommentTok{\# male\_age.hist(density=True, histtype=\textquotesingle{}stepfilled\textquotesingle{}, color=sns.desaturate("blue",.75), alpha=0.5, bins=20)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\subsection*{3. NORMAL Age with a Graduate
Degree}\label{normal-age-with-a-graduate-degree}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{3. NORMAL Age with a Graduate Degree}

The distributions of income data in the \texttt{education} groups.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{counts }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ people.groupby(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}education\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{).size()}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(counts)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Consider people with a Masters or Doctorate degree:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{graduate }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ people[(people[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}education\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{==} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Masters\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{) }\OperatorTok{|}\NormalTok{ (people[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}education\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{==} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Doctorate\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)]}
\NormalTok{sns.distplot(graduate[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}age\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{], bins}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{20}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Again, we can estimate the normal distribution based on the sample with
a graduate degree:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{mu, std }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ norm.fit(graduate[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}age\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{])}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(mu, std)}
\NormalTok{normal\_graduate\_age }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ norm(mu, std)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

How likely is \texttt{age=18} or younger in the population with a
graduate degree?

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{p }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ normal\_graduate\_age.cdf(}\DecValTok{18}\NormalTok{)}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"}\SpecialCharTok{\{:.3f\}}\StringTok{"}\NormalTok{.}\BuiltInTok{format}\NormalTok{(p))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

The chance is \(<1\%\). It is quite abnormal and suggests likely
outliers in the context of having a \texttt{graduate} degree.

\section*{Regularization}\label{sec-pr-09-regularization}

\section*{2. Data Normalization +
Reduction}\label{data-normalization-reduction}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{2. Data Normalization + Reduction}

\markright{2. Data Normalization + Reduction}

In data mining, you need to scale data to the same range to avoid
dependence on the choice of measurement units (e.g., lb vs.~kg). You
also want to obtain a reduced representation of the dataset that is much
smaller in volume but yet produces the same (or almost the same)
analytical results.

In this assignment, you will explore different options for feature
subset selection (e.g., metrics and methods) and normalization (e.g.,
z-transformation and max-min). After exploring all options, the goal is
to create the best classifier. The performance will be measured by
accuracy, the ratio of the number of correct predictions to the total
number of input samples, considering the even distribution of the class.
You are not allowed to explore any options other than feature subset
selection and normalization. For instance, you can only use one machine
learning algorithm (Logistic Regression). You cannot create a new
attribute, and so on. In this way, you can know how feature subset
selection and normalization would contribute to the modeling. It should
be noted that we are not splitting the dataset into the training set and
testing set, although the generalizability of the classifiers'
performance can be tested only with the unseen data (i.e., testing
data). We will learn about this topic later.

Please load the dataset
(\href{../data/Yelp_Usefulness_Assignment2_2.csv}{Yelp\_Usefulness\_Assignment2\_2.csv}).
There are several versions of the dataset, so please make sure that you
load the correct version.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ sklearn.linear\_model }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ LogisticRegression}
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ sklearn.metrics }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ accuracy\_score}

\CommentTok{\#\# This loads the csv file from disk}
\NormalTok{yelp\_data }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ pd.read\_csv(}
\NormalTok{    filepath\_or\_buffer }\OperatorTok{=} \StringTok{"../data/Yelp\_Usefulness\_Assignment2\_2.csv"}\NormalTok{, sep }\OperatorTok{=} \StringTok{","}\NormalTok{, header}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{ )}

\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(yelp\_data.head(}\DecValTok{20}\NormalTok{))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Then, we are going to build a baseline model by using the Logistic
Regression classifier. At the end of the following code, you can see the
accuracy of this baseline classifier (0.752). After exploring various
options for the normalization and feature subset selection, you would
want to beat this baseline model.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\#\# Create feature matrix by dropping the review\_id and label attribute}
\CommentTok{\#\# Review\_id is not going to helpful to predict the usefulness of reviews}
\NormalTok{X }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ yelp\_data.drop([}\StringTok{"review\_id"}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{"class"}\NormalTok{], }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{)    }

\CommentTok{\#\# Pre{-}processing. Sklearn takes integer as label}
\CommentTok{\#\# Create target attribute}
\NormalTok{yelp\_data[yelp\_data[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}class\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{==} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}useful\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{1}
\NormalTok{yelp\_data[yelp\_data[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}class\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{==} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}not\_useful\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{0}

\CommentTok{\#\# Specify the data type. Before specifying, the type was unknown}
\NormalTok{y }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ yelp\_data[}\StringTok{"class"}\NormalTok{].astype(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}int\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}

\CommentTok{\#\# Create a model}
\NormalTok{clf }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ LogisticRegression()}
\NormalTok{clf.fit(X, y)}

\CommentTok{\#\# predict target class based on the trained model }
\NormalTok{predictions }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ clf.predict(X)}

\CommentTok{\#\# Calculate the performance of the classifier}
\NormalTok{accuracy }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ accuracy\_score(predictions, y)}

\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(accuracy)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Here is an example to scale data using z-scale.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\#\# Apply z{-}transformation}
\NormalTok{z\_scaler }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ preprocessing.StandardScaler()}
\NormalTok{X\_scaled }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ z\_caler.fit\_transform(X)}
\NormalTok{X\_scaled }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ pd.DataFrame(X\_scaled, columns }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ X.columns)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Here is an example to scale data using max-min transformation.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\#\# Apply Min\_Max Scaler}
\NormalTok{min\_max\_scaler }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ preprocessing.MinMaxScaler()}
\NormalTok{X\_minmax }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ min\_max\_scaler.fit\_transform(X)}
\NormalTok{X\_minmax }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ pd.DataFrame(X\_minmax, columns }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ X.columns)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Here is an example of using forward feature selection with max\_min
transformation.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\#\# import the necessary libraries}
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ mlxtend.feature\_selection }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ SequentialFeatureSelector }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ SFS}

\NormalTok{min\_max\_scaler }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ preprocessing.MinMaxScaler()}
\NormalTok{X\_minmax }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ min\_max\_scaler.fit\_transform(X)}
\NormalTok{X\_minmax }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ pd.DataFrame(X\_minmax, columns }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ X.columns)}

\CommentTok{\#\# Sequential Forward Selection(sfs)}
\NormalTok{sfs }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ SFS(LogisticRegression(),}
\NormalTok{           k\_features}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{,X\_minmax.shape[}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{]),}
\NormalTok{           forward}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{, }
\NormalTok{           floating}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{False}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{           scoring }\OperatorTok{=} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}accuracy\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{           cv }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{)}

\NormalTok{sfs }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ sfs.fit(X\_minmax, y)}
\CommentTok{\#\# Get the final set of features}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(sfs.k\_feature\_names\_)}

\NormalTok{X\_selected }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ sfs.transform(X\_minmax)}

\CommentTok{\# Fit the estimator using the new feature subset}
\CommentTok{\# and make a prediction on the test data}
\NormalTok{clf.fit(X\_selected, y)}
\NormalTok{predictions }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ clf.predict(X\_selected)}

\CommentTok{\#\# Calculate the performance of the classifier}
\NormalTok{accuracy }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ accuracy\_score(predictions, y)}

\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(accuracy)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Actually, the performance would be lower than the baseline classifier,
if you use the forward feature selection with max\_min transformation.
Now, explore all possible options. For instance, you can set the
``forward'' argument of the SequentialFeatureSelector as ``False'' to
use backward feature selection. If you set the ``floating'' argument as
``True'', then you will use bi-directional stepwise feature selection.
You can also change ``scoring'' and ``cv'' options.

Please refer to the following link for the detailed arguments options:
http://rasbt.github.io/mlxtend/user\_guide/feature\_selection/SequentialFeatureSelector/\#sequential-feature-selector

Enjoy all options, find the best classifier, and answer the following
questions.

\section*{3. Association Rule Mining:
Theory}\label{association-rule-mining-theory}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{3. Association Rule Mining: Theory}

\markright{3. Association Rule Mining: Theory}

A typical example of association rule mining is market basket analysis.
This process analyzes transaction data to find associations between the
different items that customers place in their ``shopping baskets''. The
discovery of these associations can help retailers develop marketing
strategies by understanding which items are frequently purchased
together by customers. Also, online retailers can develop a
recommendation system based on association rule mining.

You are going to find association rules from the following virtual
transactional data.

{\def\LTcaptype{none} % do not increment counter
\begin{longtable}[]{@{}ll@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
Transaction ID & Items bought \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
1 & Computer, Mouse \\
2 & Computer, Tablet, Smart Phone, Smart Watch \\
3 & Computer, Smart Watch, Game Console \\
4 & Mouse, Game Console \\
5 & Tablet, Smart Watch, Smart Phone \\
6 & Smart Phone, Smart Watch \\
\end{longtable}
}

\subsection*{3.1. Rule Calculation}\label{rule-calculation}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{3.1. Rule Calculation}

3.1.1 Calculation of support (1 point)

First of all, you need to calculate the first rule, support, which is
the frequency of an itemset. You are going to use relative support,
which is the fraction of transactions that contain X itemset (i.e., the
probability that a transaction contains X itemset). If you are not
familiar with this, please watch the lecture video or read the textbook
(section 6.1). Please note that both minimum support and minimum
confidence are 50\% (i.e., 0.5).

First, you need to calculate support for frequent 1-itemsets that
consist of 1 item. If each item does not pass the minimum support
threshold, it does not need to be included for 2-itemsets candidates
that consist of 2 items (e.g., computer and mouse) according to the
Apriori property. Examine data values in the previous table and include
the calculation process. If it takes too much to use the Jupter Notebook
to write the calculation process, you can write them on paper and turn
them in as attached. Please create tables to list corresponding values:

\subsubsection*{Frequent 1-itemsets}\label{frequent-1-itemsets}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsubsection}{Frequent 1-itemsets}

3.1.2 Association Rules (1 point)

Itemsets that pass the minimum support can be used to find the
association rule X ⇒ Y. Find all the rules X ⇒ Y with minimum support
and confidence. Please see the following equations:

\[ support (A⇒B) =  P(AUB) \]

\[ confidence (A⇒B) =  P(B|A) = \frac{support(AUB)}{support(A)} \]

Please calculate the support and confidence for each rule you found and
and include the calculation process. If it takes too much to use the
Jupter Notebook to write the calculation process, you can write them on
paper and turn them in as attached. Please create a table to list
corresponding values:

{\def\LTcaptype{none} % do not increment counter
\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 8\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1481}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 8\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1605}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 8\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2222}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 8\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2346}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 8\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2346}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Rule
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
support (A⇒B)
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
confidence (A⇒B)
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Passing Minimum Support ?
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Passing Minimum Confidence ?
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
Computer ⇒ Smart Watch & .xx & .xx & Yes / No & Yes / No \\
\ldots{} & & & & \\
\end{longtable}
}

\subsection*{3.2. Pattern Evaluation
Method}\label{pattern-evaluation-method}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{3.2. Pattern Evaluation Method}

The support-Confidence framework is often limited because not all rules
found strong by this framework are interesting. Therefore, we need to
utilize other interestingness measures such as lift and chi-squared
(\(χ^2\)). You are going to calculate those other interestingness
measures from the table and compare their pros/cons.

{\def\LTcaptype{none} % do not increment counter
\begin{longtable}[]{@{}lll@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
& Smart Watch & Not Smart Watch \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
Smart Phone & 500 & 350 \\
Not Smart Phone & 100 & 50 \\
\end{longtable}
}

\[ Lift (A,B) = \frac{Confidence(A⇒B)}{Support(C)} = \frac{Support(AUB)}{Support(A)×Support(B)} \]

3.2.1 Calculation of interestingness measures (0.5 points)

Please calculate the lift and chi-squared (\(χ^2\)) for each rule and
and include the calculation process. If it takes too much to use the
Jupter Notebook to write the calculation process, you can write them on
paper and turn them in as attached. Please create a table to list
corresponding values:

{\def\LTcaptype{none} % do not increment counter
\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 8\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1481}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 8\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1605}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 8\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2222}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 8\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2346}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 8\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2346}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Rule
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
support (A⇒B)
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
confidence (A⇒B)
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Lift (A⇒B)
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
chi-squared (\(χ^2\))
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
Smart Watch ⇒ Smart Phone & .xx & .xx & .xx & xx.xx \\
Not Smart Watch ⇒ Smart Phone & .xx & .xx & .xx & xx.xx \\
\end{longtable}
}

3.2.2 Insight and Explanation (0.5 points)

If you calculated correctly, each measure might tell you different
stories. Let's assume that both minimum support and minimum confidence
are 35\% (i.e., .35). What do these interestingness measures tell you?
Please explain what each measure means. In other words, can you tell
whether there is a strong association or not? What are the pros/cons of
each measure, particularly related to this example? How are you going to
use these measures in the future according to what you learned?

\section*{4. Association Rule Mining:
Practice}\label{association-rule-mining-practice}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{4. Association Rule Mining: Practice}

\markright{4. Association Rule Mining: Practice}

You will practice with one of the advanced pattern mining topics,
quantitative association rules. So far, you have practiced with
categorical variables and calculate rules based on the contingency
table. However, relational and data warehouse data often involve
quantitative attributes and measures. For instance, in the Yelp dataset,
most attributes are not categorical variables but numeric variables. To
apply association rule mining, you can convert those attributes to
binary attributes by using either median or mean split. This is not an
ideal approach because it will lose information, but good enough for
illustration. In this assignment, you are going to use the mean split.
Based on this discretization, you will find association rules and
analyze those rules. Perhaps, these association rules can help you find
discriminative attributes to predict the usefulness of reviews. In other
words, you can use the association rule mining for feature selection.
You will need to use evaluation metrics and your own (subjective)
judgments to find meaningful associations.

\subsection*{4.1. Discretization (1
point)}\label{discretization-1-point}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{4.1. Discretization (1 point)}

Now you need to create a binary representation of attributes. We will
need to include ``class'' attribute, but drop ``review\_id'' attribute.

Here is an example of converting textual representation to numeric
representation. The library (MLXtend) you will use 1/0 representation
for binary attributes.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{yelp\_data[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}class\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{][yelp\_data[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}class\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{==} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}useful\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{1}
\NormalTok{yelp\_data[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}class\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{][yelp\_data[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}class\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{==} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}not\_useful\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{0}
\NormalTok{yelp\_data[}\StringTok{"class"}\NormalTok{].astype(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}int\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Here is an example for descretization.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{yelp\_data[}\StringTok{"degree"}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.where(yelp\_data[}\StringTok{"degree"}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{\textgreater{}=}\NormalTok{ np.mean(yelp\_data[}\StringTok{"degree"}\NormalTok{]),}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{yelp\_data[}\StringTok{"degree"}\NormalTok{].astype(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}int\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

You need to iteratively apply the above transformation for all
attributes.

Please load the dataset (Yelp\_Usefulness\_Assignment2\_2.csv). As
noted, there are several versions of the dataset, so please make sure
that you load the correct version. Convert the data by using your own
code and show the first 20 lines.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\#\# This loads the csv file from disk}
\NormalTok{yelp\_data }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ pd.read\_csv(}
\NormalTok{    filepath\_or\_buffer }\OperatorTok{=} \StringTok{"../data/Yelp\_Usefulness\_Assignment2\_2.csv"}\NormalTok{, sep }\OperatorTok{=} \StringTok{","}\NormalTok{, header}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{ )}

\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(yelp\_data.head(}\DecValTok{20}\NormalTok{))}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(yelp\_data.shape)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]

\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\section*{Recovered activity}\label{sec-lab-clustering}

\markright{Recovered activity}

\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced jigsaw, arc=.35mm, bottomrule=.15mm, bottomtitle=1mm, breakable, colback=white, colbacktitle=quarto-callout-note-color!10!white, colframe=quarto-callout-note-color-frame, coltitle=black, left=2mm, leftrule=.75mm, opacityback=0, opacitybacktitle=0.6, rightrule=.15mm, title=\textcolor{quarto-callout-note-color}{\faInfo}\hspace{0.5em}{Historical source and execution note}, titlerule=0mm, toprule=.15mm, toptitle=1mm]
