\chapter*{Association Rule Mining}\label{ch-lab-association-rules}
\addcontentsline{toc}{chapter}{Association Rule Mining}

\markboth{Association Rule Mining}{Association Rule Mining}

\section*{Recovered activity}\label{sec-lab-association-rules}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Recovered activity}

\markright{Recovered activity}

\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced jigsaw, arc=.35mm, bottomrule=.15mm, bottomtitle=1mm, breakable, colback=white, colbacktitle=quarto-callout-note-color!10!white, colframe=quarto-callout-note-color-frame, coltitle=black, left=2mm, leftrule=.75mm, opacityback=0, opacitybacktitle=0.6, rightrule=.15mm, title=\textcolor{quarto-callout-note-color}{\faInfo}\hspace{0.5em}{Historical source and execution note}, titlerule=0mm, toprule=.15mm, toptitle=1mm]

This activity was recovered from
\texttt{demo/python\_association\_rule\_mining.ipynb}. Its code is
preserved but is not executed during the public book build, so readers
can inspect it without requiring legacy package versions. Download the
\href{../notebooks/association-rules.ipynb}{source notebook} to run and
modernize it interactively.

\end{tcolorbox}

\subsection*{Introduction}\label{introduction-1}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{Introduction}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  A typical example of association rule mining is market basket
  analysis.
\item
  This process analyzes transaction data to find associations between
  the different items that customers place in their ``shopping
  baskets''.
\item
  The discovery of these associations can help retailers develop
  marketing strategies by understanding which items are frequently
  purchased together by customers.
\item
  Also, online retailers can develop a recommendation system based on
  association rule mining.
\end{itemize}

In this exercise, we are going to utilize a dataset downloaded from
Kaggle (https://www.kaggle.com/irfanasrullah/groceries). Please see the
data description on the site, if you are interested in details. Please
note that the file format is somewhat different from what we used to be
familiar with. Tuples contain itemsets purchased together in a
transaction. Therefore, each tuple contains a different number of
attributes, i.e., items. The ultimate goal is to find strong
associations among items. I left up to six items per transaction on
purpose. If there were more than six items, I deleted them. Let's open
the data. There are 9,835 tuples.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ pandas }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ pd}
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ numpy }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ np}
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ mlxtend.frequent\_patterns }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ apriori, association\_rules}
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ matplotlib.pyplot }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ plt}

\CommentTok{\#\# This loads the csv file from disk}
\NormalTok{basket\_data }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ pd.read\_csv(}\StringTok{"../data/groceries/groceries\_modified.csv"}\NormalTok{, sep }\OperatorTok{=} \StringTok{","}\NormalTok{, header }\OperatorTok{=} \VariableTok{None}\NormalTok{ )}

\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(basket\_data.head(}\DecValTok{20}\NormalTok{))}
\CommentTok{\#\# print the dimension of the data}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(basket\_data.shape)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

You can see a lot of NaN from the Pandas dataframe. It's because they
were empty in the original data. You may be interested in how many
unique items are actually in the data.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{items }\OperatorTok{=} \BuiltInTok{list}\NormalTok{()}
\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ attr }\KeywordTok{in} \BuiltInTok{range}\NormalTok{(basket\_data.shape[}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{]):}
\NormalTok{    items.extend(}\BuiltInTok{list}\NormalTok{(basket\_data[attr].unique()))}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\BuiltInTok{set}\NormalTok{(items))}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\BuiltInTok{len}\NormalTok{(}\BuiltInTok{set}\NormalTok{(items)))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\subsection*{Preprocessing}\label{preprocessing}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{Preprocessing}

There are 168 items in total that made up the entire data. Let's find
some interesting associations between them!

Before starting the fun part, we need to preprocess the dataset. The
library (MLXtend) requires 1/0 representation for attributes. With that
being said, the shape of the dataframe should be changed. As there are
168 unique items, the number of columns should be 168. In tuples, the
values should represent whether each item occurs or not in the
transaction. If an item is in the basket, it should be represented as 1.
If not, it should be represented as 0.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# Create empty list to contain the converted data}
\NormalTok{converted\_vals }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ []}
\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ index, row }\KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ basket\_data.iterrows():}
\NormalTok{    labels }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ \{\}}
    \CommentTok{\# Find items that do not occur in the transaction}
\NormalTok{    not\_occurred }\OperatorTok{=} \BuiltInTok{list}\NormalTok{(}\BuiltInTok{set}\NormalTok{(items) }\OperatorTok{{-}} \BuiltInTok{set}\NormalTok{(row))}
    \CommentTok{\# Find items that occur in the transaction}
\NormalTok{    occurred }\OperatorTok{=} \BuiltInTok{list}\NormalTok{(}\BuiltInTok{set}\NormalTok{(items).intersection(row))}
    \ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ nc }\KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ not\_occurred:}
\NormalTok{        labels[nc] }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{0}
    \ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ occ }\KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ occurred:}
\NormalTok{        labels[occ] }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{1}
\NormalTok{    converted\_vals.append(labels)}

\NormalTok{converted\_basket }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ pd.DataFrame(converted\_vals)}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(converted\_basket.head())}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

If you look at the table carefully, you will find one unwelcomed guest,
``NaN''. It was included to process empty values, now plays like a real
value. We should drop this. You see the importance of data
preprocessing.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{converted\_basket }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ converted\_basket.drop([np.nan], }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{)  }
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(converted\_basket.head())}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\subsection*{Implementation}\label{implementation}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{Implementation}

Now It's time for fun. You will create association rules between
attributes. You need to change minimum support and/or other parameters
to filter out less interesting rules.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{frequent\_itemsets }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ apriori(converted\_basket, min\_support}\OperatorTok{=}\FloatTok{0.3}\NormalTok{, use\_colnames}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{)}

\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(frequent\_itemsets.head())}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

You got no frequent itemsets. You have relatively large transactions, so
having high support is challenging. You have to tune this parameter to
get a good number of candidates.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{frequent\_itemsets }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ apriori(converted\_basket, min\_support}\OperatorTok{=}\FloatTok{0.04}\NormalTok{, use\_colnames}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{)}

\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(frequent\_itemsets)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{rules }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ association\_rules(frequent\_itemsets, metric}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"lift"}\NormalTok{, min\_threshold}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{)}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(rules)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

We can filter the dataframe. Let's look for cases whose lift is larger
than 2 and confidence is larger than 0.3.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{rules[ (rules[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}lift\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{\textgreater{}=} \DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{) }\OperatorTok{\&}\NormalTok{ (rules[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}confidence\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{\textgreater{}=} \FloatTok{0.3}\NormalTok{) ]}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\subsection*{Visualization}\label{visualization}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{Visualization}

Now It might be helpful to visualize the result. The visualization will
help you understand how each rule performs.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{plt.scatter(rules[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}support\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{], rules[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}confidence\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{], alpha}\OperatorTok{=}\FloatTok{0.7}\NormalTok{)}
\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ i }\KeywordTok{in} \BuiltInTok{range}\NormalTok{(rules.shape[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{]):}
\NormalTok{    plt.text(rules.loc[i,}\StringTok{"support"}\NormalTok{], rules.loc[i,}\StringTok{"confidence"}\NormalTok{], }\BuiltInTok{str}\NormalTok{(i))}
\NormalTok{plt.xlabel(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}support\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.ylabel(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}confidence\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.title(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Support vs Confidence\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{plt.show()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Now, you are ready to apply association rule mining to find interesting
patterns. Please find more details about the libraries you used at:
http://rasbt.github.io/mlxtend/user\_guide/frequent\_patterns/apriori/
and
http://rasbt.github.io/mlxtend/user\_guide/frequent\_patterns/association\_rules/.

\markright{Recovered activity}

\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced jigsaw, arc=.35mm, bottomrule=.15mm, bottomtitle=1mm, breakable, colback=white, colbacktitle=quarto-callout-note-color!10!white, colframe=quarto-callout-note-color-frame, coltitle=black, left=2mm, leftrule=.75mm, opacityback=0, opacitybacktitle=0.6, rightrule=.15mm, title=\textcolor{quarto-callout-note-color}{\faInfo}\hspace{0.5em}{Historical source and execution note}, titlerule=0mm, toprule=.15mm, toptitle=1mm]

Please think about tentative ideas for your final project, which will
account for \(24\%\) of your final grade.

This is a group project so please find other students to work together
(\textbf{up to four} students).

Please \textbf{choose one} of the following project types:

\section*{I. Data Mining and Report}\label{i.-data-mining-and-report}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{I. Data Mining and Report}

\markright{I. Data Mining and Report}

Find an \textbf{application domain} where you can use apply the methods
of data mining for knowledge discovery (see topics above). Use real (not
hypothetical) \textbf{data} and problems related to your interests.
While there are various types of data you can use, make sure there are
sufficient data for your problems.

General \textbf{topics of data mining} you can think about: +
Generalization: integration, aggregation, summariation, etc. +
Association and correlation analysis: frequent patterns, rules +
Classification: training and testing (predictions) + Cluster analysis:
major patterns, themes, discovery of new categories + Outlier analysis:
outliers, noise or exception + Sequence, trend and evolution analysis +
Graph mining, network analysis, web mining, etc.

The focus is on your mining \emph{processes, analysis, and report}.

\section*{II. Algorithm
Implementation}\label{ii.-algorithm-implementation}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{II. Algorithm Implementation}

\markright{II. Algorithm Implementation}

Pick a specific \textbf{data mining method} and implement it as a piece
of software that can be tested with data. You can develop the method in
Python or any other languages. The method (algorithm) can be any process
in:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Data cleaning
\item
  Data integration
\item
  Data selection
\item
  Data transformation
\item
  Data mining
\item
  Pattern evaluation
\item
  Knowledge representation
\end{enumerate}

The focus is your \emph{design, code, test, and brief documentation}.

\section*{III. Literature Review}\label{iii.-literature-review}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{III. Literature Review}

\markright{III. Literature Review}

If you are interested in a specific \textbf{research} area in data
mining, you may choose to do a literature review about it. For this type
of project, your survey of the area, critical review, and writing will
be the focus of your work. There are a wide range of topics that you can
review (see above). You can consult the textbook and related revenues
such as KDD, CIKM, etc for additional references.

We do not cover everything in this class so feel free to explore.

\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Group Submission}

\chapter*{Reinforcement Learning
Extension}\label{ch-lab-reinforcement-learning-extension}
\addcontentsline{toc}{chapter}{Reinforcement Learning Extension}

\markboth{Reinforcement Learning Extension}{Reinforcement Learning
Extension}

\section*{Recovered
activity}\label{sec-lab-reinforcement-learning-extension}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Recovered activity}

\markright{Recovered activity}

\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced jigsaw, arc=.35mm, bottomrule=.15mm, bottomtitle=1mm, breakable, colback=white, colbacktitle=quarto-callout-note-color!10!white, colframe=quarto-callout-note-color-frame, coltitle=black, left=2mm, leftrule=.75mm, opacityback=0, opacitybacktitle=0.6, rightrule=.15mm, title=\textcolor{quarto-callout-note-color}{\faInfo}\hspace{0.5em}{Historical source and execution note}, titlerule=0mm, toprule=.15mm, toptitle=1mm]

This activity was recovered from \texttt{research/rl\_taxi.ipynb}. Its
code is preserved but is not executed during the public book build, so
readers can inspect it without requiring legacy package versions.
Download the
\href{../notebooks/reinforcement-learning-extension.ipynb}{source
notebook} to run and modernize it interactively.

\end{tcolorbox}

\section*{Motivation}\label{motivation}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Motivation}

\markright{Motivation}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Classification of individual data instances
\item
  More complex problems:

  \begin{enumerate}
  \def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
  \tightlist
  \item
    No clearly defined class labels
  \item
    Better or worse, instead of true or false
  \item
    Learning from a sequence of trials
  \end{enumerate}
\end{itemize}

In supervised learning or classification, we focus on an individual data
instance at a time and try to make a prediction or decision based on
features of that single instance. While we may have built a model based
on a large number of training samples, testing is conducted more or less
on an isolated data instance, where the result of other test data has no
impact.

In reality, however, we have many situations and problems that are much
more complex than what can be treated as classification for a number of
reasons. For one, there might not be a clearly defined set of labels or
training data for supervised learning. Perhaps, it is not possible to
tell whether an action or decision is right (true) or wrong (false)
under the current circumstance. Rather, it should be focused on what is
better (improving) or worse. So the objective has more to do with
optimaization than classification.

More important, it normally takes more than one trials to figure out
what is working vs.~what is not. So whatever learning model we employ
for tasks will have to analyze a sequence of actions and returns to
identify an effective model.

Example, a robot learning to play soccer: + When to dribble? When to
pass a ball? + Timing is key, e.g.~a sequence of (teamwork) actions
leading to a goal

Imagine if you are a striker, the success of your \textbf{actions}
(e.g.~passing, dribbling, and scoring) depends on your interactions with
the \textbf{environment}, for example: + Who has the ball and where the
ball is going + Where your teammates are or running toward + Where
players on the other team are and/or moving to \ldots{}

It is a very dynamic environment that is constantly changing and you
need to be alert about what may happen next so you can be there in the
right place at the right moment, e.g.~to receive the ball and score.

\section*{Reinforcement Learning}\label{reinforcement-learning}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Reinforcement Learning}

\markright{Reinforcement Learning}

The soccer game illustrates several important concepts in Reinforcement
Learning:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ graphviz }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ Digraph}
\NormalTok{dot }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ Digraph(comment}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"Reinforcement Learning"}\NormalTok{, graph\_attr}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{\{}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}rankdir\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{:}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}LR\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{\}, engine}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}dot\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{dot.node(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}A\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Agent\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, shape}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}circle\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\CommentTok{\# with dot.subgraph(name=\textquotesingle{}child\textquotesingle{}, node\_attr=\{\textquotesingle{}shape\textquotesingle{}: \textquotesingle{}box\textquotesingle{}\}) as c:}
\CommentTok{\#     c.node(\textquotesingle{}c\textquotesingle{}, \textquotesingle{}Action\textquotesingle{}, shape=\textquotesingle{}box\textquotesingle{})}
\CommentTok{\#     c.node(\textquotesingle{}s\textquotesingle{}, \textquotesingle{}State\textquotesingle{}, shape=\textquotesingle{}egg\textquotesingle{})}
\CommentTok{\#     c.node(\textquotesingle{}r\textquotesingle{}, \textquotesingle{}Reward\textquotesingle{}, shape=\textquotesingle{}egg\textquotesingle{})}
\NormalTok{dot.node(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}E\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Environment\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, shape}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}circle\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\CommentTok{\# dot.edge(\textquotesingle{}A\textquotesingle{}, \textquotesingle{}c\textquotesingle{})}
\CommentTok{\# dot.edges([\textquotesingle{}Ac\textquotesingle{}, \textquotesingle{}cE\textquotesingle{}, \textquotesingle{}sA\textquotesingle{}, \textquotesingle{}rA\textquotesingle{}, \textquotesingle{}Es\textquotesingle{}, \textquotesingle{}Er\textquotesingle{}])}
\NormalTok{dot.edge(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}A\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}E\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Action\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{dot.edge(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}E\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}A\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}State\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{dot.edge(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}E\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}A\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Reward\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{dot}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{Agent} is a software program, just like a soccer player, who
  learns and adapts to perform \texttt{Actions}, e.g.~to play in a
  soccer game.
\item
  \texttt{Environment} refers to the entire dynamically changing setting
  where agents operate, e.g.~the soccer field, players, referees, and
  their interactions under the rules.
\item
  \texttt{State} of the environment such as the location of the ball and
  moving speeds of players.
\item
  \texttt{Reward} or \texttt{Penalty} as a result of an agent's action.
  For example, there are big rewards such as scoring a goal or penalties
  such as receiving a red card; but there are also minor rewards such as
  passing the ball accurately and being able to have longer procession,
  which may lead to opportunities to score.
\end{enumerate}

\section*{Q-Learning}\label{q-learning}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Q-Learning}

\markright{Q-Learning}

Through actions and interactions (state and reward) with the
environment, it is possible for an agent to gain knowledge and improve
its actions. We refer to as

\begin{quote}
The \texttt{Policy}: the method (strategy) an agent uses to act on its
knowledge to maximize its long-term rewards.
\end{quote}

\texttt{Q-Learning} is a reinforcement learning policy, in which the
agent keeps track of \textbf{rewards} associated with each
\textbf{state} and \textbf{action}.

\subsection*{Q-Table}\label{q-table}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{Q-Table}

Specifically, the agent stores rewards in a so-called \textbf{Q-table}
like this:

{\def\LTcaptype{none} % do not increment counter
\begin{longtable}[]{@{}llllll@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
& \(A_1\) & \(A_2\) & \(A_3\) & .. & \(A_m\) \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\(S_1\) & 0 & 0 & 0 & & 0 \\
\(S_2\) & 0 & 0 & 0 & & 0 \\
.. & 0 & 0 & 0 & & 0 \\
\(S_n\) & 0 & 0 & 0 & & 0 \\
\end{longtable}
}

where: + \(S_1, S_2, .., S_n\) are all possible states (rows). +
\(A_1, A_2, .., A_m\) are all possible actions (columns) for each state.

The Q-table is initiated with \(0\) values and gets updated whenever the
agent performs an action and receives a reward (or penalty) from the
environment.

Suppose at time \(t\), the agent is at the state of \(s_t\), performs an
action \(a_t\) and receives a reward of \(r_t\).

Here is one strategy to update the Q-table:

\begin{eqnarray}
Q'(s_t, a_t) & \leftarrow & (1-\beta) Q(s_t, a_t) + \beta (r_t + \gamma \max_a{Q(s_{t+1},a)}) 
\end{eqnarray}

where: + \(\beta \in [0,1]\) is the learning rate. A greater \(\beta\)
leads to more aggressive updating. + \(\gamma \in [0,1]\) is the
discount factor for future rewards. A greater \(\gamma\) tends to put
more emphasis on long-term rewards.

\subsection*{Exploration
vs.~Exploitation}\label{exploration-vs.-exploitation}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{Exploration vs.~Exploitation}

An agent learns from trials and errors. So learning requires (random)
\textbf{exploration} in the environment. At some points, the agent may
gain ``sufficient'' knowledge to make wiser decisions based on a trained
Q-table. However, in order to allow opportunities to continued learning,
one should avoid complete \textbf{exploitation} of Q-table for action
selection.

For the tradeoff, we introduce a third parameter \(\epsilon\)
(probability), at time \(t\):

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  With \(\epsilon\) probability, the agent selects a random action.
\item
  With \(1 - \epsilon\) probability, the agent selects the action with
  the maximum reward for state \(s_t\) in the Q-table:
  \(a_t = \underset{a}{\operatorname{argmax}}{r(s_t,a)}\)
\end{enumerate}

\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Taxi Cab}

The \texttt{Taxi} task in OpenAI's \texttt{gym} is a simplified problem
of driving a taxi cab in city blocks.

Here is an visualization of city blocks, more like a parking lot:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ gym}
\NormalTok{env }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ gym.make(}\StringTok{"Taxi{-}v3"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{env.reset()}
\NormalTok{out\_text }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ env.render(mode}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"ansi"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{out\_html }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ str2html(out\_text)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{display(HTML(out\_html))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\subsection*{Problem}\label{problem}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{Problem}

Major tasks of the taxi cab (agent) include:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Pick up a passenger from a location (in blue color), at R, G, B, or Y.
\item
  Drop off the passenger at the target location (in red), at R, G, B, or
  Y.
\item
  Travel as little as possible to complete the tasks.
\end{enumerate}

In each state, the cab can take any of the following actions:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\setcounter{enumi}{-1}
\tightlist
\item
  Move south
\item
  Move north
\item
  Move east
\item
  Most west
\item
  Pickup passenger
\item
  Dropoff passenger
\end{enumerate}

There are related rewards and penalties for each action:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \(-1\) point penalty for each action/move (just like gasoline
  consumption).
\item
  \(-10\) points penalty for illegal pickup or dropoff.
\item
  \(20\) points reward for a successful delivery of the passenger.
\end{itemize}

For example, given the following \texttt{state} where the cab is to: 1.
Pick up passenger at pickup location (blue); 2. Drive to the destination
at destination (red); 3. Drop off the passenger.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{env.reset()}
\NormalTok{env.s }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{328}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{display(HTML(str2html(env.render(mode}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"ansi"}\NormalTok{))))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\subsection*{Action without Learning}\label{action-without-learning}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{Action without Learning}

How can the agent (cab) learn to perform the tasks?

We will first start with a dummy verion where the cab: + simply selects
a random action + and does not learn

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{    action }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ env.action\_space.sample()}
\NormalTok{    state, reward, done, info }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ env.step(action)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{epochs }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{0}
\NormalTok{penalties, reward }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{0}
\NormalTok{frames }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ []}
\NormalTok{done }\OperatorTok{=} \VariableTok{False}

\ControlFlowTok{while} \KeywordTok{not}\NormalTok{ done: }
\NormalTok{    action }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ env.action\_space.sample()}
\NormalTok{    state, reward, done, info }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ env.step(action)}
    \ControlFlowTok{if}\NormalTok{ reward }\OperatorTok{==} \OperatorTok{{-}}\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{: }
\NormalTok{        penalties }\OperatorTok{+=} \DecValTok{1}
        
\NormalTok{    frames.append(\{}
        \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}frame\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{: env.render(mode}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}ansi\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{), }
        \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}state\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{: state, }
        \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}action\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{: action, }
        \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}reward\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{: reward}
\NormalTok{    \})}
    
\NormalTok{    epochs }\OperatorTok{+=} \DecValTok{1}

\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Timesteps taken: }\SpecialCharTok{\{\}}\StringTok{"}\NormalTok{.}\BuiltInTok{format}\NormalTok{(epochs))}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Penalties incurred: }\SpecialCharTok{\{\}}\StringTok{"}\NormalTok{.}\BuiltInTok{format}\NormalTok{(penalties))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{print\_frames(frames, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\FloatTok{0.2}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

You can tell that the cab is fooling around and its moves and actions
are random and meaningless. By looking at the behavior of the cab, we
can hardly have confidence in it to develop any intelligence and to
perform a better job.

The strategy of random actions does not work!

\subsection*{Explore and Learn}\label{explore-and-learn}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{Explore and Learn}

Now let's follow the Q-Learning idea so the agent (cab) can learn from
its failures and successes. In particular, we would like the agent to
remember what works vs.~what does not, that is:

What are past \texttt{rewards} (or penalties) for each \texttt{action}
taken in each \texttt{state}.

We know there are \(6\) different actions the cab can select from. But
how many states are there?

Here are factors to be considered in the combinations of states:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  The cab can be at one of the \(5\times5\) blocks.
\item
  The passenger can be at one of the \(4\) pickup locations (RGBY) or in
  the cab: \(\times5\).
\item
  The cab is to drop off the passenger at one of the \(4\) locations:
  \(\times4\).
\end{enumerate}

The total number of states: \(5\times5\times5\times4=500\).

So we need to create a Q-table of \(500\) rows (states) \(\times\) \(6\)
columns (actions):

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ numpy }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ np}
\NormalTok{q\_table }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.zeros([env.observation\_space.n, env.action\_space.n])}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

We start with a Q-table like this (first 10 states):

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{q\_table[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{:}\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{]}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Each row has \(6\) columns because there is one value for each of the
\(6\) actions. We only show first 10 states here but remember there are
\(500\) rows (states) in total.

Now we will let the agent (cab) explore a little bit, with
\(\epsilon=0.1\).

Given the current \texttt{state}, the agent has: + \(10\%\) probability
to explore and select random actions.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{action }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ env.action\_space.sample()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \(90\%\) probability to select the best action based on the Q-table
  (memory)
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{action }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.argmax(q\_table[state])}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

After the action, the agent (cab) receives a \texttt{reward} and updates
its Q-table:

\begin{eqnarray}
Q'(s_t, a_t) & \leftarrow & (1-\beta) Q(s_t, a_t) + \beta (r_t + \gamma \max_a{Q(s_{t+1},a)}) 
\end{eqnarray}

where: + \(\beta = 0.1\) is the learning rate + \(\gamma = 0.6\) is the
discount for future rewards + \(Q(s_t, a_t)\) is the value in Q-table
for state \(s_t\) and action \(a_t\) + \(r_t\) is the the received
reward (or penalty) + \(Q'(s_t, a_t)\) denotes the updated value in
Q-table

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\OperatorTok{\%\%}\NormalTok{time}
\CommentTok{"""Agent under training"""}

\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ random}
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ IPython.display }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ clear\_output}

\CommentTok{\# Hyperparameters}
\NormalTok{a }\OperatorTok{=} \FloatTok{0.1} \CommentTok{\# alpha}
\NormalTok{g }\OperatorTok{=} \FloatTok{0.6} \CommentTok{\# gamma}
\NormalTok{e }\OperatorTok{=} \FloatTok{0.1} \CommentTok{\# epsilon}

\CommentTok{\# keeping track of}
\NormalTok{all\_epochs }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ []}
\NormalTok{all\_penalties }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ []}

\CommentTok{\# Train the cab for 100,000 tasks}
\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ i }\KeywordTok{in} \BuiltInTok{range}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{10}\OperatorTok{**}\DecValTok{5}\OperatorTok{+}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{):}
\NormalTok{    state }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ env.reset()}
\NormalTok{    epochs, penalties, reward }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{0}
\NormalTok{    done }\OperatorTok{=} \VariableTok{False}
    
    \ControlFlowTok{while} \KeywordTok{not}\NormalTok{ done: }
        \ControlFlowTok{if}\NormalTok{ random.uniform(}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{) }\OperatorTok{\textless{}}\NormalTok{ e: }
\NormalTok{            action }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ env.action\_space.sample() }\CommentTok{\# random exploration}
        \ControlFlowTok{else}\NormalTok{: }
\NormalTok{            action }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.argmax(q\_table[state]) }\CommentTok{\# act on knowledge}
        
\NormalTok{        next\_state, reward, done, info }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ env.step(action)}
\NormalTok{        next\_max }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.}\BuiltInTok{max}\NormalTok{(q\_table[next\_state])}
        
\NormalTok{        old\_value }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ q\_table[state, action]}
\NormalTok{        new\_value }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ (}\DecValTok{1} \OperatorTok{{-}}\NormalTok{ a) }\OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{ old\_value }\OperatorTok{+}\NormalTok{ a }\OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{ (reward }\OperatorTok{+}\NormalTok{ g }\OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{ next\_max)}
\NormalTok{        q\_table[state, action] }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ new\_value}
        
        \ControlFlowTok{if}\NormalTok{ reward }\OperatorTok{==} \OperatorTok{{-}}\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{: }
\NormalTok{            penalties }\OperatorTok{+=} \DecValTok{1}
            
\NormalTok{        state }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ next\_state}
\NormalTok{        epochs }\OperatorTok{+=} \DecValTok{1}
    \ControlFlowTok{if}\NormalTok{ i }\OperatorTok{\%} \DecValTok{100} \OperatorTok{==} \DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{: }
\NormalTok{        clear\_output(wait}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{)}
        \BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\SpecialStringTok{f"Episode: }\SpecialCharTok{\{}\NormalTok{i}\SpecialCharTok{\}}\SpecialStringTok{"}\NormalTok{)}

\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Agent is trained!"}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

After training the agent (cab) for: + \(100,000\) tasks + about 1 minute
on a Mac

we obtain the following Q-table:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{np.set\_printoptions(precision}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{)}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(q\_table[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{:}\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{])}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

The values are different from the initial values but how useful are
they?

\subsection*{Act on Knowledge}\label{act-on-knowledge}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{Act on Knowledge}

Let's test the trained model (with an updated Q-table) for \(100\) tasks
(episodes):

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{total\_epochs, total\_penalties }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{0}
\NormalTok{episodes }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{100}
\NormalTok{trained\_frames }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ []}

\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ \_ }\KeywordTok{in} \BuiltInTok{range}\NormalTok{(episodes):}
\NormalTok{    state }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ env.reset()}
\NormalTok{    epochs, penalties, reward }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{1}
    
\NormalTok{    done }\OperatorTok{=} \VariableTok{False}
    
    \ControlFlowTok{while} \KeywordTok{not}\NormalTok{ done: }
\NormalTok{        action }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.argmax(q\_table[state])}
\NormalTok{        state, reward, done, info }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ env.step(action)}
        
\NormalTok{        trained\_frames.append(\{}
            \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}frame\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{: env.render(mode}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}ansi\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{), }
            \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}state\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{: state, }
            \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}action\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{: action, }
            \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}reward\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{: reward}
\NormalTok{        \})}
        
        \ControlFlowTok{if}\NormalTok{ reward }\OperatorTok{==} \OperatorTok{{-}}\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{: }
\NormalTok{            penalties }\OperatorTok{+=} \DecValTok{1}
            
\NormalTok{        epochs }\OperatorTok{+=} \DecValTok{1}
        
\NormalTok{    total\_penalties }\OperatorTok{+=}\NormalTok{ penalties}
\NormalTok{    total\_epochs }\OperatorTok{+=}\NormalTok{ epochs}

\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\SpecialStringTok{f"After }\SpecialCharTok{\{}\NormalTok{episodes}\SpecialCharTok{\}}\SpecialStringTok{ spisodes:"}\NormalTok{)}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\SpecialStringTok{f"Average timesteps per episode: }\SpecialCharTok{\{}\NormalTok{total\_epochs }\OperatorTok{/}\NormalTok{ episodes}\SpecialCharTok{\}}\SpecialStringTok{"}\NormalTok{)}
\BuiltInTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\SpecialStringTok{f"Average penalties per episode: }\SpecialCharTok{\{}\NormalTok{total\_penalties }\OperatorTok{/}\NormalTok{ episodes}\SpecialCharTok{\}}\SpecialStringTok{"}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Summary of the test, After 100 episodes: + Average timesteps per
episode: 12.85 + Average penalties per episode: 0.0

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{print\_frames(trained\_frames, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{100}\NormalTok{, }\FloatTok{0.5}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\section*{How did the agent learn?}\label{how-did-the-agent-learn}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{How did the agent learn?}

\markright{How did the agent learn?}

Example, \texttt{State\ 0}:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Cab location: \((2, 3)\)
\item
  Pickup location \texttt{B}: \(3\)
\item
  Dropoff location \texttt{G}: \(1\)
\end{itemize}

How does the cab learn to go \texttt{south} first to maximize its
long-term reward?

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{env.encode(}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\subsection*{Branches}\label{branches}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{Branches}

At each state, there are \(6\) actions to perform, which can be
visualized as:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ matplotlib.pyplot }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ plt}
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ networkx }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ nx}

\NormalTok{G }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ nx.balanced\_tree(}\DecValTok{6}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{names }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ \{}
    \DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{: }\StringTok{"State"}\NormalTok{,}
    \DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{: }\StringTok{"South"}\NormalTok{,}
    \DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{: }\StringTok{"North"}\NormalTok{,}
    \DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{: }\StringTok{"East"}\NormalTok{,}
    \DecValTok{4}\NormalTok{: }\StringTok{"West"}\NormalTok{,}
    \DecValTok{5}\NormalTok{: }\StringTok{"Pickup"}\NormalTok{,}
    \DecValTok{6}\NormalTok{: }\StringTok{"Dropoff"}
\NormalTok{\}}

\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\NormalTok{plt.show()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

By taking an action, the agent (possibly) moves to another state and,
from there, can select another action from the six.

This can be visualized as new branches from each one of the actions
taken:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{G }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ nx.balanced\_tree(}\DecValTok{6}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{names }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ \{}
    \DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{: }\StringTok{"State"}\NormalTok{,}
    \DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{: }\StringTok{"South"}\NormalTok{,}
    \DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{: }\StringTok{"North"}\NormalTok{,}
    \DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{: }\StringTok{"East"}\NormalTok{,}
    \DecValTok{4}\NormalTok{: }\StringTok{"West"}\NormalTok{,}
    \DecValTok{5}\NormalTok{: }\StringTok{"Pickup"}\NormalTok{,}
    \DecValTok{6}\NormalTok{: }\StringTok{"Dropoff"}
\NormalTok{\}}

\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]

\NormalTok{plt.show()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

As the agent goes down the path, the number of potential choices and
actions increase exponentially. How does the agent get rewarded for one
of the most efficient path?

In particular, how does the agent maximize its \textbf{long-term award}
by moving \texttt{south\ first} in the following step?

The path is equivalent to:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ show\_graph():}
\NormalTok{    G }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ nx.path\_graph(}\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    names }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ \{}
        \DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{: }\StringTok{"0"}\NormalTok{,}
        \DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{: }\StringTok{"1.South"}\NormalTok{,}
        \DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{: }\StringTok{"2.South"}\NormalTok{,}
        \DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{: }\StringTok{"3.Pickup"}\NormalTok{,}
        \DecValTok{4}\NormalTok{: }\StringTok{"4.East"}\NormalTok{,}
        \DecValTok{5}\NormalTok{: }\StringTok{"5.North"}\NormalTok{,}
        \DecValTok{6}\NormalTok{: }\StringTok{"6.North"}\NormalTok{,}
        \DecValTok{7}\NormalTok{: }\StringTok{"7.North"}\NormalTok{,}
        \DecValTok{8}\NormalTok{: }\StringTok{"8.North"}\NormalTok{,}
        \DecValTok{9}\NormalTok{: }\StringTok{"9.Dropoff"}
\NormalTok{    \}}

\NormalTok{    positions }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ \{}
        \StringTok{"0"}\NormalTok{: (}\DecValTok{30}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{60}\NormalTok{),}
        \StringTok{"1.South"}\NormalTok{: (}\DecValTok{30}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{40}\NormalTok{),}
        \StringTok{"2.South"}\NormalTok{: (}\DecValTok{30}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{20}\NormalTok{),}
        \StringTok{"3.Pickup"}\NormalTok{: (}\DecValTok{40}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{20}\NormalTok{),}
        \StringTok{"4.East"}\NormalTok{: (}\DecValTok{50}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{20}\NormalTok{),}
        \StringTok{"5.North"}\NormalTok{: (}\DecValTok{55}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{30}\NormalTok{),}
        \StringTok{"6.North"}\NormalTok{: (}\DecValTok{60}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{40}\NormalTok{),}
        \StringTok{"7.North"}\NormalTok{: (}\DecValTok{65}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{50}\NormalTok{),}
        \StringTok{"8.North"}\NormalTok{: (}\DecValTok{70}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{60}\NormalTok{),}
        \StringTok{"9.Dropoff"}\NormalTok{: (}\DecValTok{75}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{70}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    \}}

\NormalTok{    colors }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ [}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}0.75\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}0.75\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}0.75\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}lightblue\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}0.75\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}0.75\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}0.75\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}0.75\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}0.75\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}orange\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{]}

\NormalTok{    G }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ nx.relabel\_nodes(G, names)}
\NormalTok{    plt.figure(figsize}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{12}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{6}\NormalTok{))}
\NormalTok{    plt.xlim(}\DecValTok{25}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{80}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    plt.ylim(}\DecValTok{10}\NormalTok{,}\DecValTok{90}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    nx.draw(G, with\_labels}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{, pos}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{positions, node\_size}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{2200}\NormalTok{, node\_color}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{colors, alpha}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    plt.show()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{show\_graph()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

For the sake of discussion, suppose values in Q-table are all the same
\(0\).

By taking the action \texttt{south} at state \texttt{0}: 1. The agent
receive a penalty of \(-1\). 2. It will update its Q-value for
\texttt{state\ 0} with action \texttt{south}.

\begin{eqnarray}
Q'(0, south) \
& \leftarrow & (1-\beta) Q(0, south) + \beta (r_t + \gamma \max_a{Q(s_{t+1},a)}) \\
         & = & 0.9 \times 0 + 0.1\times(-1 + 0.6\times 0)\\
         & = & - 0.1
\end{eqnarray} - 0.1 \textbackslash end\{eqnarray\}

Given: + Learning rate: \(\beta = 0.1\) + Future award discount:
\(\gamma = 0.6\)

So every \(state \to action\) on the shown path will receive the same
penalty of \(-1\) and updated with a new value of \(-0.1\) in the
Q-table. One may think with this penalty along the path, the agent may
not favor similar actions in the future.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{show\_graph()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

However, when the cab reaches to the destination and selects an
\texttt{dropoff} action at \texttt{state\ 8}:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  It receives a reward of \(20\)
\item
  It updates its Q-values for action \texttt{dropoff} at state
  \texttt{8}.
\end{enumerate}

\begin{eqnarray}
Q'(8, dropoff) \
& \leftarrow & (1-\beta) Q(8, dropoff) + \beta (r_t + \gamma \max_a{Q(s_{t+1},a)}) \\
         & = & 0.9 \times 0 + 0.1\times(20 + 0.6\times 0)\\
         & = & 2
\end{eqnarray} 2 \textbackslash end\{eqnarray\}

With the new updated value of \(2\) for a \texttt{dropoff} at state
\texttt{8}, the agent will favor this specific action at this specific
state in the future. But how about the previous actions at previous
states leading to the dropoff? How do they get rewarded as well?

Remember in the Q-table updating policy, we have:

\begin{eqnarray}
Q'(s_t, a_t) & \leftarrow & (1-\beta) Q(s_t, a_t) + \beta (r_t + \gamma \max_a{Q(s_{t+1},a)}) 
\end{eqnarray}

For example, next time, when an action at state \texttt{7} is being
updated:

\begin{eqnarray}
Q'(7, North) 
& \leftarrow & ... + \beta \gamma \max_a{Q(s_{t+1}, a)} \\
         & = & ... + 0.1\times0.6 \max_a{Q(s_{t+1}, a)}
\end{eqnarray}

Because: 1. Action \texttt{north} at state \texttt{7} leads to state
\texttt{8} 2. The most rewarding action at state \texttt{8} is to
\texttt{dropoff}, with Q-value \(2\)

To update \(Q'(7, north)\), the future reward becomes: \begin{eqnarray}
0.06 \max_a{Q(s_{t+1}, a)} 
& = & 0.06 \max_a{Q(8,a)} \\
& = & 0.06 \times Q(8, dropoff) \\
& = & 0.06 \times 2
\end{eqnarray}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{show\_graph()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

The main takeaway here is that a reward at one state will be
``backpropagated'' to an earlier \(state, action\) leading to that
favorable state. With a sufficient number of iterations to train the
agent, the rewards or penalties will be carried all the way back to even
earlier state-actions.

In the end, the reward received for \texttt{dropoff} at state \texttt{8}
will have an impact on going \texttt{south} at state \texttt{0}, even
though they are only remotely connected. Overall, the Q-learning policy
will help improve the taxi cab's behavior toward greater long-term
rewards.

\subsection*{Comparison}\label{comparison}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{Comparison}

Let's compare the dummy cab (random model) and the trained model side by
side:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{compare\_frames(}\StringTok{"Random Model"}\NormalTok{, frames, }\StringTok{"Trained Model"}\NormalTok{, trained\_frames, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{200}\NormalTok{, }\FloatTok{0.5}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\subsection*{Conclusion}\label{conclusion}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{Conclusion}

It is easy to tell that while the random model continues to lose and
accumulate penalties, the trained model is much smarter in knowing how
to move directly to pickup and dropoff locations to maximize its
rewards.

Applications of Q-learning certainly go beyond the simplified taxi cab
example here. A recent example is the AlphaGo that defeated the best
human player. One can also imagine a similar reinforcement learning
policy to be used by a smart floor vaccum to maximize the amount of dirt
it can pick up to make your rooms clean.
